Definition and Ruling on Water Rights and Major Rivers
Opening definition and ruling on water rights for great rivers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on great rivers, explain the condition of public harm, and identify the hadith-based proof.

Core Idea
Great rivers are common property—anyone may use them as long as they do not harm others.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational ruling on water rights in the Hanafi school: great rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates are not owned by anyone. The passage establishes that everyone has the right to draw water for irrigation, drinking, wudu, etc., subject to the condition of no public harm. This ruling is anchored in the hadith of collective partnership in water, pasture, and fire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
The passage begins by defining 'shurb' (شرب) as the share or right to water for land. Note that technically it refers to the right to irrigate land, not just drinking.
Linguistically, shurb can mean 'drink', but legally it is a specific right attached to land.
2
State the ruling on great rivers
The text explicitly states: 'the great rivers are not owned' (الأنهار العظام غير مملوكة). This is the central ruling—no individual or entity owns great rivers.
This applies to rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates, not to small privately dug canals.
3
List the permitted uses
The passage enumerates several permissible actions: irrigating land, performing wudu, drinking, installing a watermill, and digging a channel from the river to one's land.
The list is not exhaustive but covers common needs.
4
Identify the condition
All these uses are allowed 'provided it does not harm the public' (إن لم يضر بالعامة). Harm includes reducing water flow, causing flooding, or blocking access.
This condition is critical—without it, the use is prohibited.
5
Locate the dalil and explain
The ruling is based on the hadith: 'People are partners in three: water, pasture, and fire' (الناس شركاء في ثلاث: الماء والكلأ والنار). The general partnership implies common ownership, so no one can claim exclusive rights over great rivers.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qat'i dalalah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل الشرب وهو نصيب الماء الأنهار العظام كدجلة الفرات غير مملوكة ولكل أن يستقي أرضه ويتوضا به ويشرب وينصب الرحا عليه ويكري نهراً منها إلى أرضه إن لم يضر بالعامة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «الناس
Issues of water rights (shurb): It is the share of water. The great rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates are not owned, and everyone may irrigate his land, perform wudu with it, drink, install a watermill, and dig a channel from it to his land, provided it does not harm the public, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'People...'
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage opens the section on water rights (shurb) in the commentary. It first defines shurb: linguistically it means the share of water, as in the Quran: 'each shurb present' (54:28). Technically, it is the right to water for land, not for other purposes. The author then states a key ruling: great rivers such as the Tigris and Euphrates are not owned by anyone. Therefore, anyone may take water from them for irrigation, drinking, wudu, and even installing a watermill or digging a channel, as long as it does not harm the general public. This is based on the hadith: 'People are partners in three things: water, pasture, and fire.' The hadith is incomplete in the text but is well-known. This ruling distinguishes between owned and unowned water sources.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): The share or right to water, specifically for irrigating land.
أنهار عظام (anhār ʿiẓām): Great rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates, which are not owned by any individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on great rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates according to the Hanafi school?
A) They are owned by the state.
B) They are not owned, and anyone may use them for irrigation and other needs without public harm.
C) They are owned by the adjacent landowners.
D) They are divided into shares among the public.

Key Takeaway
Great rivers are common property; individuals may use them but must avoid public harm.

Shared Rights in Water, Fire, and Herb
Explaining the hadith on shared rights and conditions for use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on common water, fire, and herb and identify the condition for permissible use.

Core Idea
Unenclosed water, fire, and herb are common; they become private property by enclosure.

The famous hadith declares people partners in water, fire, and herb. But what does this mean exactly? Does it forbid private ownership? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi interpretation that these resources are common only until enclosed, and that using them is conditional on not harming the public.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its literal meaning
The hadith says 'People are partners in three: water, fire, and herb.' Read the source passage to see how the commentator treats it.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud and other collections.
2
Understand the Hanafi restriction: applies only to unenclosed items
The commentary explains that 'water' means unenclosed water (e.g., river water, not water in a container). Similarly, fire and herb are common only when not enclosed or collected.
Enclosure (iḥrāz) is the method of acquiring ownership of permissible things (mubāḥāt).
3
Recognize the condition: no harm to the public
Using common resources is permitted only if it does not harm the general public. The text gives examples: digging a channel is allowed unless it harms others; building a mill that causes harm is forbidden.
This condition is derived from the principle of la ḍarar.
4
Apply the ruling to concrete examples
Work through the examples in the passage: drawing water from a river vs. taking water from a private well; kindling fire from a campfire vs. taking an ember from someone's private fire; cutting grass from the wild vs. from a fenced field.
The analogy with game (ṣayd) helps illustrate the transition from common to private ownership.
5
Summarize the legal effect
Finally, state the ruling: using unenclosed water, fire, and herb is permissible (mubāḥ) as long as it does not harm the public. Once enclosed, they become private property and cannot be used without permission.
This is the muftā bihī position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطعه شركاء في ثلاث في الماء والنار والكلا) (۱) ولأن هذه الأنهار ليس لأحذ فيها يد على لأن قهر الماء يمنع قهر غيره فلا يكون محرزاً في الملك بالإحراز، فإذا لم يكن مملوكاً كان مشتركاً. والمراد بالماء في الحديث ما ليس بمحرز فإن المحرز قد ملكه فخرج عن كونه مباحاً كالصيد إذا أحرزه لا يجوز لأحد أن ينتفع به إلا بإذنه. وشرط لجواز الانتفاع أن لا يضر بالعامة فإن كان يضر بالعامة ليس له الكري ونصب الرحا لأن الانتفاع بالمباح لا يجوز إلا إذا كان لا يضر بالعامة كالشمس والقمر والهواء والمراد بالكلأ الحشيش الذي ينبت بنفسه من غير أن ينبته أحد ومن غير أن يزرعه ويسقيه فيملكه من وأحرزه، وإن كان في أرض غيره. والمراد بالنار والاستضاءة بنورها والاصطلاء بها والإيقاد من لهبها فليس لأحد أن يمنع من ذلك. إذا كان في الصحراء بخلاف ما لو أراد أن يأخذ جمرة لأنه ملكه ويتضرر بذلك فكان له منعه كسائر أملاكه ا هـ .
He said: they are partners in three: water, fire, and herb. And because these rivers, no one has a hand over them because the force of water prevents the force of others, so it is not enclosed (muḥriz) in ownership by enclosure (iḥrāz). If it is not owned, it is shared. And what is meant by water in the hadith is that which is not enclosed, for the enclosed has been owned and leaves being permissible like game when caught; no one may use it without permission. And the condition for permissibility of use is that it does not harm the public; if it harms the public, he may not dig or install a mill because using the permissible is only allowed if it does not harm the public, like the sun, moon, and air. And what is meant by herb (kalā') is grass that grows by itself without being planted or watered by anyone, so he owns it by enclosing it, even if it is on someone else's land. And what is meant by fire is using its light, warming oneself, and kindling from its flame; no one may prevent that if it is in the desert, unlike if he wants to take an ember because he owns it and would be harmed, so he may prevent it like his other possessions.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This is a ruling-with-proof passage from the Sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains the famous hadith that people are partners in three things: water, fire, and herb. The commentator clarifies that partnership applies only to unenclosed (non-owned) water, fire, and herb. Once any of these is enclosed or collected, it becomes private property and cannot be used without permission. For example, water in a container is owned. The condition for using the common items is that it must not cause harm to the public. Thus, one may draw water from a river but not build a dam that harms others. Similarly, one may use fire's light and warmth from a campfire in the desert, but cannot take a burning ember from someone's private fire. Grass that grows naturally is common until someone encloses it by harvesting or otherwise claiming it. The commentary uses analogies to game (şayd) to illustrate the transition from common to private ownership.

Key Terms
شركاء (shurakā'): Partners in ownership, meaning common property shared by all people.
محرز (muḥriz): Enclosed or collected, making something private property through physical possession or enclosure.
كلأ (kalā'): Natural grass that grows without human cultivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition may one use common water, fire, or herb?
A) Without any condition, they are common for all.
B) Only if it is not harmful to the public.
C) Only during travel.
D) Only if the owner gives permission.

Key Takeaway
Unenclosed water, fire, and herb are common property, but they become privately owned upon enclosure; use of common items is permissible only if it does not harm the public.

Rights in Owned Water Sources: Drinking, Watering Animals, and Limits
Ruling with proof and expansions on water rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the rights of travelers to use water from owned sources, identify the conditions under which the owner may prevent access, and apply the distinction between drinking/watering animals and irrigation.

Core Idea
Necessity grants travelers the right to drink and water animals from owned water, but irrigation is excluded to protect the owner's benefit.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a crucial area of Hanafi fiqh: the tension between private ownership of water sources and the need of travelers. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states the basic rule, and the commentary expands with proofs and exceptions. Understanding this ruling helps you appreciate how Shari'a balances property rights with communal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Start with the matn: what is the ruling for each person (including travelers) regarding owned rivers, wells, and ponds? The text states they have the right to a drink and to water their animals, but not to irrigate their land.
The ruling is mubah (permissible) with a right attached due to necessity.
2
Understand the basis (dalil)
The commentary provides two proofs: first, a narration (hadith) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed this; second, rational reasoning: these water sources are not like enclosed property; they are initially mubah (ownerless) and ownership arises only through enclosure (ihraz). A traveler cannot carry enough water, so necessity permits taking what is needed.
The dalil is based on both naqli (narrated) and aqli (rational) arguments.
3
Distinguish between drinking/watering animals and irrigation
Why is irrigation not allowed? Because it has no limit; the traveler could take all the water, destroying the owner's benefit. Drinking and watering animals are limited by need. Irrigation would cause definite harm to the owner, whereas animal watering typically does not.
This distinction is key to the ruling.
4
Examine the exception: harm to the water source
If the traveler's use of water for animals actually damages the riverbank (e.g., many cattle), the owner can prevent even that use. The text says: 'If damage to the river is feared due to many cattle, he prevents.' This shows that the right is conditional on no harm.
Harm to property overrides the traveler's right.
5
Review expansions from al-Hidayah and al-Muhit
Al-Hidayah: the traveler may drink even if it consumes all the water. Al-Muhit: if the owner wants to charge a fee or entry, he can prevent only if the traveler has an alternative. For water dug in dead land (mawat), the owner cannot prevent entry as long as no damage is done. Also, a farmer cannot stop someone from watering his animals even if water is exhausted.
These cases reinforce the priority of essential needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وفي الأنهار المملوكة والآبار والحياض لكل شربه وسقي دوابه لا أرضه وإن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرة البقور يمنع وإنما كان له حق الشرب وسقي الدواب لما روينا ولأن الأنهار والآبار والحياض لم توضع للإحراز والمباح لا يملك إلا بالإحراز ولكن المسافر لا يمكنه أن يأخذ ما يوصله إلى مقصده فيحتاج أن يأخذ مما يمر عليه مما ذكر ما يحتاج إليه لنفسه ودوابه وصاحبه، فلو منع من ذل لحقه ضرر عظيم وهو مدفوع شرعاً بخلاف سقي الأراضي حيث يمنع وإن لم يكن فيه ضرر لأن في إباحة ذلك إبطال حق صاحب الأنهار إذ لا نهاية لذلك فتذهب منفعة صاحب الأنهار فيلحقه بذلك ضرر بخلا سقي الدواب لأنه مثله لا يلحقه به ضرر حتى لو تحقق فيه الضرر يمنع وهو المراد بقوله وإن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرة البقور لأن الحق لصاحبه على الخصوص، وإنما أثبتنا ما ذكرنا لغيره للضرورة فلا معنى لابقائه على وجه يضر بصاحبه قال في الهداية : ولهم الشرب وإن شربوا الماء كله ا هـ. وفي المحيط : ولو أراد صاحب الأرض أن يغرف باجرة فلصاحب الملك أن من الدخول وإن لم يجد يقال لصاحب الملك إما أن يعطيه الماء وإما أن تمكنه من الدخول بشرط أن لا يكسرها في النهر. قالوا هذا إذا كان في أرض مملوكه، فأما إذا حفر في أرض موات لم يكن لصاحب النهر منعه من الدخول إذا كان لا يكسر مسناة النهر لأن الأرض كانت مشتركة بين الناس كافة فأما إذا أحياها إنسان لم تنقطع الشركة في الدخول لأهل الشفعة، ويجوز أن تكون رقبة الشي لإنسان وللآخر فيه حق الدخول ا هـ. أيضاً : رجل له ماء يجري إلى مزرعته فيجيء رجل ويسقي دوابه حتى ينفذ الماء كله هل لصاحب النهر أن يمنعه؟ ليس له ذلك اهـ.
He said: (And in owned rivers, wells, and ponds, each has a drink and watering of his animals, not his land; and if damage to the river is feared due to many cattle, he prevents). And he only has the right to drink and water animals because of what we narrated and because rivers, wells, and ponds are not made for enclosure, and the permissible is not owned except by enclosure, but the traveler cannot take what will reach his intended place, so he needs to take from what he passes by of what is mentioned as needed for himself, his animals, and his companion. So if he were prevented from that, he would suffer great harm, which is prevented by Shari'a, unlike irrigating land where he is prevented even if there is no harm, because permitting that would nullify the right of the owner of the river since there is no end to it, so the owner's benefit would be lost and he would be harmed. But watering animals is like him, no harm comes to him; even if harm is actualized, he is prevented, and that is what is meant by his saying 'and if damage to the river is feared due to many cattle' because the right belongs to its owner specifically. We only established what we mentioned for others due to necessity, so there is no point in keeping it in a way that harms its owner. Al-Hidayah says: And they have the drink even if they drink all the water. And in al-Muhit: If the owner of the land wants to draw water for a fee, the owner of the river may prevent entry; and if he does not find (water), the owner of the river is told either to give him water or to allow him entry on condition that he does not break the riverbank. They said this if it is on owned land; but if he dug in dead land (mawāt), the owner of the river cannot prevent him from entering if he does not break the riverbank because the land was common property among all people; and if a person revives it, the partnership in entry does not end for the people of pre-emption (shuf'a). And it is permissible that the corpus of a thing belongs to one person and another has the right of entry. Also: A man has water flowing to his farm; another man comes and waters his animals until all the water runs out: can the owner of the river prevent him? He does not have that.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This is a ruling-with-proof passage from the Sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the rights of travelers to use water from owned rivers, wells, and ponds. The matn states that everyone has the right to drink and water animals from such sources, but not to irrigate land. If there is a risk of damage to the river from many cattle, the owner can prevent even the watering of animals. The commentary explains the rationale: the necessity of the traveler outweighs the owner's exclusive right for drinking and animal watering, but irrigation would consume indefinite water and destroy the owner's benefit. Al-Hidayah is quoted saying that even if the traveler drinks all the water, it is permissible. Al-Muhit discusses cases of controlling access, such as when the owner wants to charge for drawing water—he may prevent entry if the traveler has his own way to get water. If the river is dug in dead land, the owner cannot prevent entry as long as the riverbank is not damaged. Additionally, a specific case is cited: a man with water flowing to his farm cannot prevent another from watering his animals even if all water is consumed. This shows the priority of drinking and animal needs over irrigation rights.

Key Terms
شربة (shurbah): A drink, meaning the amount of water sufficient for a person to drink.
سقي (saqy): Watering animals, distinct from irrigating land.
تخريب (takhrīb): Damage or destruction, here referring to the riverbank being damaged by many cattle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for allowing a traveler to drink from an owned river?
A) The river is common property.
B) Necessity removes the owner's exclusive right.
C) The owner's consent is always implied.
D) Rivers are never owned.

Key Takeaway
Necessity permits drinking and watering animals from owned water sources, but irrigation is not allowed as it destroys the owner's benefit.

Ownership of Stored Water and Legal Consequences of Unauthorized Use
Ruling on stored water ownership, theft, and fighting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of water enclosed in containers, identify the shubha preventing hadd for theft, and explain the conditions under which a person may fight for water.

Core Idea
Water stored in containers is privately owned; using it without permission is haram, but the hadd penalty is lifted by shubhat shirkah.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on water that has been collected and stored in vessels. While such water becomes private property, a shade of partnership (shubhat shirkah) from a hadith creates legal ambiguity that prevents the application of criminal penalties. The text also addresses the right to fight for water in extreme need, distinguishing between flowing water and stored water, and discusses the permissibility of carrying water for irrigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on stored water ownership
Recognize that water enclosed in a container (kuz, hubb) is owned by the person who enclosed it, based on the principle of iḥrāz. Without permission, using it is not allowed.
The analogy is to captured game (ṣayd).
2
Analyze the effect of shubhat shirkah
The hadith 'people are partners in water' introduces a doubt of partnership. This doubt does not negate ownership but prevents the application of the ḥadd punishment for theft and similar penalties.
The text includes an objection and answer regarding the general verse: the hadith is specific, not contradicting the Book or other legal proofs.
3
Examine conditions for fighting for water
If a person fears death from thirst and the water is from a flowing source (river/well), he may fight the owner with a weapon if the owner intends to kill. For stored water, he may only fight without a weapon and only if the owner has excess, similar to famine rules.
The text from al-Kafi suggests fighting without a weapon is preferable (firstā), implying weapon use is permissible but not recommended.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on carrying water
The majority allow carrying water from a river to one's house for watering plants without the owner's permission, based on common practice. Some scholars of Bukhara require permission.
The text declares the majority view as 'al-awwal aṣaḥḥ' (the first is more correct).
5
Synthesize practical outcomes
Ownership of stored water is protected, but penalties are relaxed due to doubt. The right to fight is limited by the type of water and necessity. Permissibility of carrying water for use is guided by custom.
This illustrates how Hanafi fiqh balances property rights with necessity and common practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والمحرز في الكوز والحب لا ينتفع فيه إلا بإذن صاحبه) لأنه ملكه بالإحراز فكان أحق به كالصيد إذا أخذه لكن فيه شبهة الشركة لظاهر ما روينا فيعمل فيما يسقط بالشبهة، ولو سرق الماء في موضع يعز فيه الماء وهو يساوي نصاباً لا يقطع . واعترض عليه بأنه على هذا ينبغي أن لا يقطع في شيء من الأشياء كلها لأن قوله تعالى هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً) [البقرة : ٢٩] يورث الشبهة بهذا الطريق. وأجيب بأن العمل بالحديث يوافق قوله تعالى هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ولا يلزم من العمل به إبطال الكتاب خلاف قوله تعالى هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً فإن العمل به على الإطلاق يبطل العمل بقوله تعالى الزانية والزاني ﴾ [النور: ٢] والسارق والسارقة [المائدة: ٣٨] وغير ذلك، فدل على أن المراد به غير ما دل عليه الخصوصيات؛ كذا في العناية. واعترض بأنه وإن لم يلزم من العمل بالحديث إبطال الكتاب لكن يلزم به إبطال دليل شرعي آخر فإنكم حكمتم بأن الماء المحرز في الأواني يصير مملوكاً بالإحراز وينقطع حق الغير عنه وهو حكم شرعي لا بد له من دليل شرعي لا محالة، فلو عملنا بالحديث المذكور على الإطلاق لزم إبطال ذلك الدليل الشرعي فدل على أن المراد بالحديث المذكور غير ما دل عليه بخصوص الدليل الشرعي الدال على أن الماء المحرز في الأواني ملك مخصوص لمحرزه، ولو الشفعة كانت البئر أو الحوض أو النهر في ملك رجل فله أن يمنع من يريد من الدخول، وقد قدمنا عن المحيط بتفاصيله، وحكم الكلأ حكم الماء على التفاصيل المتقدمة. ولو منع رب الماء وهو يخاف على نفسه أو على دابته العطش فإن له أن يقاتله بالسلاح لأثر النهر من يريد عمر، ولأنه قصد إتلافه. وإن كان الماء محرزاً في الأواني فليس للذي يخاف العطش أن يقاتل بالسلاح. وله أن يقاتله بغير السلاح إذا كان فيه فضل عن صاحبه فصار نظير الطعام حالة المخمصة. وفي الكافي : قيل في البئر ونحوه والأولى أن يقاتله بغير السلاح لأنه ارتكب معصية فصار بمنزلة التعزير ؛ هذا يشير ألى أن له أن يقاتله بالسلاح حيث جعل الأولى أن لا يقاتله به وأهل الشفعة بأن كانوا يشربون الماء كله بأن كان نهراً صغيراً وفيما يرد عليه من المواشي كثرة ينقطع الماء اختلفوا فيه ؛ قال بعضهم ليس لربه أن يمنع وأكثرهم على أن له لأنه يلحقه الضرر بذلك فصار كسقي الأرض وله أن يأخذ منه الماء للوضوء وغسل يمنع الثياب في الأصح وقيل ينقلهما في النهر. ولو أراد أن يسقي شجراً أو خضراً في داره فحمل الماء إليه بالجرة كان له ذلك. وقال بعض أئمة بخارى : ليس له ذلك إلا بإذن صاحب النهر ؛ والأول أصح لأن الناس يتوسعون في ذلك. وليس له أن نخله يسقي وشجره وأرضه
He said: (And what is enclosed in a jug or a vessel may not be used except with the permission of its owner) because he owns it by enclosure, so he is more entitled to it like game when caught, but there is a shade of partnership (shubhat shirkah) due to the apparent meaning of what we narrated, so it acts to prevent punishment where there is a doubt. And if someone steals water in a place where water is scarce and it is worth a nisab, the hand is not cut. An objection was raised: on this basis, the hand should not be cut for anything, because Allah's saying 'He created for you all that is in the earth' establishes doubt in this way. The answer: acting upon the hadith agrees with the verse and does not entail abrogation of the Book, unlike if we act on the verse generally it would abrogate verses on punishment. So it indicates the verse is restricted by specific proofs. Another objection: even if the hadith does not abrogate the Book, it abrogates another legal proof that water in containers becomes owned by enclosure. The answer: the hadith is specific to unenclosed water, not contradictory. And if a well, basin, or river is owned by a person, he can prevent anyone from entry, as previously detailed from al-Muhit, and the ruling on herb is like that of water. And if the owner of water prevents someone who fears thirst for himself or his animal, he may fight him with a weapon for the flowing river, because he intends to destroy him. But if the water is enclosed in containers, the one fearing thirst may not fight with a weapon, but he may fight without a weapon if the owner has excess, similar to the case of food in famine. In al-Kafi: it is said regarding a well and the like that it is better to fight without a weapon because the owner committed a sin, so it is like tazir. This indicates he can fight with a weapon since he said 'better not' implies it is permissible. And the people of pre-emption: if they drink all the water because it is a small river and many cattle come upon it cutting off water, there is disagreement; some say the owner may not prevent, but most say he may because he is harmed, so it is like irrigating land. And he may take water for ablution and washing clothes in the correct view; some say he must carry them to the river. And if he wants to water trees or vegetables in his house and carries water in a jar, he may do so. Some scholars of Bukhara said: he may not do so without the owner's permission; but the first is more correct because people are lenient in that. And he may not water his palm trees, trees, and land.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This is a ruling-with-proof passage from the Sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It covers three main issues: (1) Water stored in containers (kuz, hubb) is privately owned and may not be used without permission; there is a doubt of partnership due to the hadith that people are partners in water, but this doubt only prevents penalties, not ownership. (2) Theft of such water does not incur hand amputation because of that doubt; the text includes an objection and answer regarding the verse 'He created for you all that is on earth' and the hadith about partnership, concluding that the hadith is specific. (3) The right to fight for water: if a person fears death from thirst and the water is in a river or well, he may fight with a weapon if the owner intends to kill him; but if the water is stored in containers, he may only fight without a weapon and only if the owner has excess, similar to the rule for food in famine. The commentary also discusses cases of wells, pre-emption, and watering trees. The last part mentions an intra-Hanafi difference: some scholars of Bukhara held that carrying water to one's house for trees requires the river owner's permission, but the majority allow it due to common practice.

Key Terms
محرز (muḥriz): Enclosed or collected, here specifically water stored in a container like a jug or vessel.
شبهة شركة (shubhat shirkah): Doubt of partnership, meaning a legal ambiguity arising from the hadith that people are partners in water.
مخمصة (makhmaṣah): Famine or extreme hunger, used as an analogy for extreme thirst.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not cut off the hand of a thief who steals water from a container in a place where water is scarce?
A) Because stealing water is always permissible.
B) Because the value of water never reaches the nisab.
C) Because there is a doubt (shubha) of partnership due to the hadith on sharing water.
D) Because water is not considered property.

Key Takeaway
Water stored in containers is privately owned, but the hadd penalty for theft is lifted by shubhat shirkah, and the right to fight for water is limited by the type of source and necessity.

Ownership of Water in Containers and the Effect of Doubt on Hadd
Ruling with proof, objection, and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water in containers, explain why theft does not incur the hadd penalty due to shubha, and evaluate the objections and responses from the passage.

Core Idea
Water in containers is owned by its preserver, but a doubt of common ownership prevents amputation for theft.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a nuanced fiqh issue: can water collected in vessels become private property, and if stolen, does the thief lose his hand? The Hanafi school answers both affirmatively but with a crucial nuance — the hadd penalty is averted by a legal doubt (shubha) arising from the general permissibility of water in its natural state. The commentary then defends this position against two objections, demonstrating the interplay between general and specific texts in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on water in containers
The matn states: water preserved in a pitcher or jar cannot be used without the owner's permission. This establishes ownership through ihraz (preservation), like game caught.
The ruling is sahih (valid) — ownership is effective.
2
Understand the basis of ownership: ihraz
Ownership comes from physically taking possession of something ownerless (mubah). Water in its natural state is mubah, but once collected, it becomes owned.
This is analogous to capturing game or gathering firewood.
3
Recognize the doubt (shubha) from the hadith
The hadith 'Water, herbage, and fire are shared' creates a doubt that water might be common property. This doubt does not negate ownership but prevents the extreme penalty of amputation.
The hadith is overridden by specific evidence for property rights, but the doubt remains for penal consequences.
4
Apply the doubt to theft: no amputation
If someone steals water from a container in a place where water is scarce and its value reaches the nisab, the hand is not cut because the doubt (shubha) blocks the hadd.
Hanafis apply shubha broadly to avert hudud.
5
Analyze the objections and responses
Two objections are raised: (1) If this doubt applies to water, why not to all property? (2) The doubt itself contradicts the ruling of ownership. The responses use usul principles: general verses are qualified by specific texts, and acting on the hadith does not abrogate the Book but is restricted by specific legal evidence.
This shows the Hanafi method of reconciling conflicting evidences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والمحرز في الكوز والحب لا ينتفع فيه إلا بإذن صاحبه) لأنه ملكه بالإحراز فكان أحق به كالصيد إذا أخذه لكن فيه شبهة الشركة لظاهر ما روينا فيعمل فيما يسقط بالشبهة، ولو سرق الماء في موضع يعز فيه الماء وهو يساوي نصاباً لا يقطع . واعترض عليه بأنه على هذا ينبغي أن لا يقطع في شيء من الأشياء كلها لأن قوله تعالى هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً [البقرة : ٢٩] يورث الشبهة بهذا الطريق. وأجيب بأن العمل بالحديث يوافق قوله تعالى هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ولا يلزم من العمل به إبطال الكتاب خلاف قوله تعالى هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً فإن العمل به على الإطلاق يبطل العمل بقوله تعالى الزانية والزاني [النور: ٢] والسارق والسارقة [المائدة: ٣٨] وغير ذلك، فدل على أن المراد به غير ما دل عليه الخصوصيات؛ كذا في العناية. واعترض بأنه وإن لم يلزم من العمل بالحديث إبطال الكتاب لكن يلزم به إبطال دليل شرعي آخر فإنكم حكمتم بأن الماء المحرز في الأواني يصير مملوكاً بالإحراز وينقطع حق الغير عنه وهو حكم شرعي لا بد له من دليل شرعي لا محالة، فلو عملنا بالحديث المذكور على الإطلاق لزم إبطال ذلك الدليل الشرعي فدل على أن المراد بالحديث المذكور غير ما دل عليه بخصوص الدليل الشرعي الدال على أن الماء المحرز في الأواني ملك مخصوص لمحرزه.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Water preserved in a pitcher or jar cannot be used except with the permission of its owner) because he owns it by preservation, so he has more right to it, like game when he catches it. But there is a doubt of common ownership due to the apparent meaning of what we have narrated, so it is applied in that which falls due to doubt. If someone steals water in a place where water is scarce and it is worth a nisab, his hand is not cut off. It was objected: on this basis, the hand should not be cut off for anything at all, because the verse 'He created for you all that is on the earth' [2:29] creates a doubt in this way. The answer: acting on the hadith agrees with the verse 'He created for you all that is on the earth', and acting on it does not necessitate abrogating the Book, contrary to if the verse were acted upon generally, it would invalidate the verses about the adulterer and the thief, etc. So it indicates that the intended meaning of the verse is other than what the specific texts indicate; as in Al-Inayah. Another objection: even if acting on the hadith does not necessitate abrogating the Book, it necessitates abrogating another legal evidence, for you have ruled that water preserved in vessels becomes owned by preservation and the right of others is cut off—this is a legal ruling that must have legal evidence. If we acted on the aforementioned hadith generally, that legal evidence would be invalidated, so it indicates that the intended meaning of the hadith is other than what is indicated by the specific legal evidence that water in vessels is specific property of its preservator.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: water collected in containers (like pitchers and jars) becomes the private property of the one who collected it. No one may use it without the owner's permission. The commentary explains the legal basis: ownership through physical possession (ihraz), analogous to capturing game. However, there is a legal doubt (shubha) due to the general permissibility of water in its natural state, based on a hadith. This doubt affects the application of the hadd punishment for theft: the thief's hand is not cut off even if the water's value reaches the nisab, because the doubt prevents the application of the limit. The author then addresses two objections. First, if this doubt always negates the hadd, then why not apply it to all property? The answer distinguishes between the general verse (Quran 2:29) and specific verses on theft and adultery; the general verse is qualified by specifics. Second, the doubt itself might contradict the ruling of ownership in containers, but the response clarifies that the hadith is not absolute; it is overridden by specific legal evidence establishing ownership. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that a legal ruling can exist despite a potential doubt, and that doubt only prevents penal consequences, not the substantive property right.

Key Terms
المحرز (al-muhraz): Water that has been collected and preserved in containers, thus falling under private ownership.
الشبهة (al-shubha): A legal doubt arising from conflicting evidence (general permissibility vs. specific ownership) that prevents the application of hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that the hand of a thief is not cut off for stealing water from a container, even if the value reaches the nisab?
A) Because water is not considered property.
B) Because there is a legal doubt (shubha) due to the general permissibility of water.
C) Because the container itself is not stolen.
D) Because the nisab for water is different.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of water through ihraz is valid, but the hadd for theft is averted due to shubha from the general permissibility of water.

Water Rights: Private Wells, Emergency Fighting, and Shared Rivers
Series of rulings on water rights, emergency fighting, and shared rivers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on private water rights, the conditions for permissible fighting to obtain water, and the rules for shared rivers and irrigation.

Core Idea
Water rights depend on ownership and necessity; life-threatening thirst justifies force but only from flowing sources.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq compiles several rulings on water rights that balance private ownership with the necessities of life and cultivation. You will learn when a water owner can exclude others, when a desperate person may use force, how partners manage shared rivers, and the limits on irrigation from another's property. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi method of applying analogical reasoning (qiyas) and considering public practice (urf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the water sources and ownership categories
The passage concludes by categorizing waters into three types: large public rivers (unowned), owned rivers/wells/pools, and water in containers. Each has distinct rulings. First, recognize that private wells and rivers give the owner the right to exclude others from entering his property (though not necessarily from the water itself in emergencies).
Ownership is key: a private water source is owned, but water in containers is even more protected.
2
Analyze the emergency fighting exception
When the water owner refuses access and the person fears death from thirst (self or animal), the passage permits fighting with a weapon if the water is from a flowing river or well. The justification is that the owner intends harm by refusing. However, if water is in containers, only non-weapon force is allowed, and only if there is surplus beyond the owner's need.
The analogy to food in famine (al-makhmasa) is used: just as one may take food to save life, one may take flowing water.
3
Distinguish between partners (al-shuf'a) and outsiders
Partners in a small river where livestock deplete the water have a dispute: some say the owner cannot prevent, but the majority hold he can because of harm. For outsiders, the owner can prevent irrigation of land or trees, but cannot prevent taking water for wudu or washing clothes in the river.
The term 'shuf'a' here refers to co-owners of water rights, not pre-emption in real estate.
4
Apply the rules for carrying water to one's property
Watering one's own trees or vegetables by carrying water in a jar is allowed without permission, according to the correct view (contrary to some Bukhara scholars who require permission). However, irrigating land from another's river requires the owner's permission.
The difference is based on custom (urf): people are lenient about small amounts carried for personal use.
5
Summarize the three categories of water and their rulings
The passage ends with a summary: great rivers (public) are owned by no one; owned rivers/wells have the rulings discussed; water in vessels is fully private and only taken under extreme necessity as per the rules above.
This categorization helps memorize the key distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو الشفعة كانت البئر أو الحوض أو النهر في ملك رجل فله أن يمنع من يريد من الدخول، وقد قدمنا عن المحيط بتفاصيله، وحكم الكلأ حكم الماء على التفاصيل المتقدمة. ولو منع رب الماء وهو يخاف على نفسه أو على دابته العطش فإن له أن يقاتله بالسلاح لأثر النهر من يريد عمر، ولأنه قصد إتلافه. وإن كان الماء محرزاً في الأواني فليس للذي يخاف العطش أن يقاتل بالسلاح. وله أن يقاتله بغير السلاح إذا كان فيه فضل عن صاحبه فصار نظير الطعام حالة المخمصة. وفي الكافي : قيل في البئر ونحوه والأولى أن يقاتله بغير السلاح لأنه ارتكب معصية فصار بمنزلة التعزير ؛ هذا يشير ألى أن له أن يقاتله بالسلاح حيث جعل الأولى أن لا يقاتله به وأهل الشفعة بأن كانوا يشربون الماء كله بأن كان نهراً صغيراً وفيما يرد عليه من المواشي كثرة ينقطع الماء اختلفوا فيه ؛ قال بعضهم ليس لربه أن يمنع وأكثرهم على أن له أن لأنه يلحقه الضرر بذلك فصار كسقي الأرض وله أن يأخذ منه الماء للوضوء وغسل يمنع الثياب في الأصح وقيل ينقلهما في النهر. ولو أراد أن يسقي شجراً أو خضراً في داره فحمل الماء إليه بالجرة كان له ذلك. وقال بعض أئمة بخارى : ليس له ذلك إلا بإذن صاحب النهر ؛ والأول أصح لأن الناس يتوسعون في ذلك. وليس له أن نخله يسقي وشجره وأرضه من نهر غيره إلا بإذن صاحبه وله أن يمنع من ذلك. فالحاصل المياه ثلاثة : الأنهار العظام التي لا تدخل في ملك أحد، والأنهار التي هي مملوكة، وما صار في الأواني وقد ذكرنا حكم كل واحد بتوفيق الله تعالى.
And if a well, pool, or river is in the ownership of a man, he may prevent whoever wants to enter. We have already presented from Al-Muhit its details. And the ruling of herbage is like the ruling of water on the aforementioned details. And if the owner of water refuses while he fears for himself or his animal from thirst, then he may fight him with a weapon due to the effect of the river from him who wants to cultivate, and because he intends to destroy him. And if the water is preserved in vessels, then the one who fears thirst may not fight with a weapon. But he may fight him without a weapon if there is surplus beyond the owner's need, so it becomes similar to food in a famine. In Al-Kafi: it was said regarding the well and similar: it is better to fight without a weapon because he committed a sin, so it is like a discretionary punishment. This indicates that he may fight with a weapon, since he stated that it is better not to fight with it. And the partners (shuffa) in a case where they all drink the water until it is depleted, if it is a small river and the livestock brought to it are many so that the water stops, they differed about it: some said the owner may not prevent, but the majority hold that he may prevent because harm befalls him by that, so it becomes like irrigating land. And he may take water from it for wudu and washing, but he may not prevent clothes in the correct view, and it was said he removes them in the river. And if he wants to water trees or vegetables in his house, carrying water to it in a jar, that is allowed. Some scholars of Bukhara said: it is not allowed except with permission of the owner of the river; the first is more correct because people are lenient about that. And he may not water his palm trees, trees, and land from another's river except with its owner's permission; and he may prevent that. In summary, waters are three: great rivers that do not enter anyone's ownership, owned rivers, and what is in vessels. We have mentioned the ruling of each one by the help of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage compiles several rulings on water rights. First, if a well, pool, or river is privately owned, the owner can prevent others from entering his property. Second, if the water owner refuses access and someone fears death from thirst, the desperate person may fight with a weapon to obtain water from a flowing river or well, because the owner's refusal intends harm. However, if the water is in containers, fighting with weapons is not allowed; only non-weapon force is permissible if there is surplus, analogous to food in extreme hunger. An opinion from Al-Kafi recommends non-weapon fighting as a form of discretionary punishment (ta'zir) for the owner's sin. Third, for partners in a small river where livestock deplete the water, the majority allow the owner to prevent overuse due to harm. Fourth, one may take water for wudu and washing, but cannot prevent washing clothes in the river. Fifth, carrying water in jars for irrigating one's trees is permitted even without permission, according to the correct view. The passage concludes by categorizing water into three types: large public rivers (non-owned), owned rivers, and water in containers, each with distinct rulings.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Partners or co-owners in a shared water source (river, well) who have rights to water usage.
الكلأ (al-kala'): Herbage, grass, or vegetation used as fodder; its ruling is analogous to water in the discussed issues.
المخمصة (al-makhmasa): Famine or extreme hunger; used as an analogy for the emergency water case.
التعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment; in the context, fighting without a weapon is likened to a ta'zir for the owner's sin of refusing water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When may a person fight with a weapon to obtain water from someone who refuses?
A) When the water is in a container and he fears thirst.
B) When the water is from a river or well and he fears for his life.
C) Whenever the owner refuses, regardless of the source.
D) Only if the owner attacks first.

Key Takeaway
Water rights in Hanafi fiqh balance private ownership with the necessity to preserve life, allowing force only under strict conditions linked to the water source and degree of urgency.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Cost Apportionment for Clearing a Shared River
Rulings on clearing rivers, funding, and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on cost apportionment for clearing a shared river and explain the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
For a shared river, each owner pays only for the portion up to his land according to Imam Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn say all pay proportionally.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the obligation and funding of clearing rivers. After establishing that public rivers are cleared from the treasury and private rivers by their owners, it presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: how should the cost be shared when a river is owned by multiple people? The answer depends on how we understand the benefit each owner derives from the river.

Guided Path
1
Identify the river types
First, distinguish between a non-owned (public) river and an owned (private) river. The passage specifies different rules for each: public rivers are cleared from bayt al-mal; private rivers are the responsibility of the owners.
The key funding distinction: jizya/kharaj for public, private funds for private.
2
Locate the central dispute
Find where Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ. The dispute occurs specifically for a 'shared river' (al-nahr al-mushtarak). Examine the two opposing positions on cost apportionment.
3
Analyse Imam's position and proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that each owner bears the cost only for the part of the river from its top until the water passes his land. Beyond that, he is free. His proof: the primary benefit is irrigation; once water passes, the owner no longer needs the channel for that purpose.
The secondary benefit of drainage is not equal to irrigation.
4
Analyse the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that all owners share the cost proportionally from top to bottom because each benefits from the entire river — not only for irrigation but also for drainage of excess water. A blockage anywhere harms all.
Their proof: the lower owner needs the channel to drain surplus water; otherwise his crop is damaged.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text states that the fatwa is on Imam's view, as cited from al-Khaniyya. Understand the practical consequence: in a river with three owners, according to Imam, the topmost pays for clearing the entire length, the middle pays from the top to his land (excluding the top section?), actually each pays from the top to his own land; so the topmost pays for all three sections? Wait careful: Imam says 'from its top; when it passes the land of a man, he is free.' That means each person pays for the portion from the top to his own land. So the topmost owner pays for the first section only? Actually if the river is from source to end, the first owner pays for the first section (top to his land), the second pays for the portion from top to his land (which includes the first section plus the second), and the third pays for the entire river. That seems cumulative? But the text says 'when it passes a man's land, he is free' implying that once the water has passed, he no longer contributes to clearing downstream. So the topmost pays only for the first part, the next pays for the first and second parts, and the last pays for all. That is the practical difference: according to Imam, the downstream owners bear more cost. According to Sahibayn, all pay equally per share. So the thamarah is opposite: Sahibayn's view is more equitable per benefit, Imam's view is based on primary need.
The text does not explicitly say cumulative, but logically if each pays from top to his land, the downstream owners pay for more sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وكري نهر غير مملوك من بيت المال) لأن ذلك لمصلحة العامة وبيت المال معد لها . قال في الهداية : ويصرف ذلك من الجزية والخراج دون العشر والصدقات لأن الثاني للفقراء ولأول للنوائب. قال رحمه الله : (فإن لم يكن فيه شيء يجبر الناس على كربه يعني إذا لم يكن في بيت المال شيء أجبر الإمام الناس على كربه لأن الإمام نصب ناظراً وفي تركه ضرر عظيم على الناس وقلما يتفق العوام علي المصالح باختيارهم فيجبرهم عليه لما روي أن عمر أجبر في مثل هذا فكلموه فقال : لو تركتم لبعتم أولادكم إلا أنه يخرج للكري من كان يطيق الكري منهم ويجعل مؤته على الأغنياء الذي لا يطيقون الكري بأنفسهم. قال في الهداية : فإن أراد أن يحصص النهر خوف الانتشاف وفيه ضرر عظيم يجبرهم على ذلك اهـ . قال رحمه الله : وكري) ما هو مملوك على أهله ويجبر الآبي على كربه) لأنه منفعة لهم على الخصوص فتكون مؤنته عليهم ولأن الغرم بالغنم ومن أبى منهم يجبر، وقيل إن كان خاصاً لا يجبر والفاصل بين الخاص والعام أن ما يستحق به الشفعة خاص وما لا يستحق به الشفعة عام. وبيان الفرق أنه إذا كان عاماً فيه دفع ضرر عام فيجبر الآبي بخلاف الخاص، وفي الضرر الخاص يمكن الدفع بأن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي فينفق ويرجع على الممتنع بحصته، وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو جعفر، وصار كزرع بين شريكين امتنع من الانفاق فلصاحبه أن ينفق عليه بأمر القاضي ويرجع عليه بما أنفق فكذا هذا؛ كذا في المحيط بخلاف ما إذا كان عاماً لا يمكن الرجوع لكبرهم فيجبر الممتنع، ولا يقال في كراء النهر الخاص احياء له حقوق أهل الشفعة فيكون في تركه ضرر عام لأنا نقول : لا لأجل أهل الشفعة ألا ترى أن أهل النهر لو امتنعوا عن كربه لا يجبرهم في ظاهر الرواية لأنهم امتنعوا عن عمارة أراضيهم. ولو كان حق أهل الشفعة معتبراً لا جبر. التتارخانية : معناه أن ينقلوا نصيب الآبي من الشرب مقدار ما بيلغ قيمة ما أنفق . أحدهما جبر وفي قال رحمه الله : (ومؤنة كري النهر المشترك عليهم من أعلاه فإذا جاوز أرض رجل برئ) وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : المؤنة عليهم جميعاً من أول النهر إلى آخره بالحصص لأن كل واحد منهم ينتفع بالأسفل كما ينتفع بالأعلى لأنه يحتاج إلى مسيل الفاضل من الماء فإنه إذا سد عليه فاض الماء إلى أرضه فيفسد ،زرعه ولأن كل واحد منهم ينتفع بالنهر من أوله إلى أسفله. وفي الخانية : الفتوى على قول الإمام واختلف أئمتنا في الطريق الخاص إذا احتاج الإصلاح ؛ قيل هو على هذا الاختلاف عند الإمام عليه المؤنة إلى أن يجاوز أرضه، وعندهما من أوله إلى
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And clearing a river that is not owned from the public treasury) because that is for the public interest and the public treasury is prepared for it. He said in Al-Hidayah: That is spent from the jizya and kharaj, not from the 'ushr and sadaqat because the second is for the poor and the first is for calamities. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If there is nothing in it, he forces the people to clear it) meaning if there is nothing in the public treasury, the Imam forces the people to clear it because the Imam is appointed as a supervisor, and leaving it causes great harm to the people, and rarely do the common people agree on interests voluntarily, so he forces them to do it based on what was narrated that Umar forced in such a situation and they spoke to him, so he said: 'If you leave it, you will sell your children.' Except that he employs for clearing those who are able to clear among them, and he imposes its cost on the wealthy who are not able to clear themselves. He said in Al-Hidayah: If he wants to partition the river out of fear of seepage and there is great harm in it, he forces them to do that. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And clearing what is owned (i.e., a privately owned river) is upon its owners, and the one who refuses is forced to clear it) because it is a benefit specifically for them, so its cost is upon them, and because loss goes with gain. Whoever refuses among them is forced. It was said: if it is private, he is not forced. The distinction between private and general is that whatever entitles one to pre-emption (shuf'a) is private, and whatever does not is general. The explanation of the difference: if it is general, it repels general harm, so the refuser is forced; contrary to private harm, the remedy can be that he raises the matter to the judge, who spends and then recovers from the refuser his share. The jurist Abu Ja'far adopted this, and it is like a crop between two partners where one refuses to spend; the partner may spend with the judge's order and recover what he spent. So is this; as in Al-Muhit, contrary to a general case where recovery is not possible due to their large numbers, so the refuser is forced. And it cannot be said that clearing a private river revives the rights of the pre-emptors and thus leaving it causes general harm, because we say: no, for the sake of the pre-emptors—do you not see that if the people of the river refuse to clear it, they are not forced in the apparent narration because they refuse to cultivate their own lands. If the right of pre-emptors were considered, they would not be forced. In Al-Tatarkhaniyya: its meaning is that they transfer the share of the refuser from the water to the amount of the value of what he spent. One says forced? And he said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the cost of clearing the shared river is upon them from its top; when it passes the land of a man, he is free) and this is according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the cost is upon all of them from the beginning to the end in proportion, because each one benefits from the lower part as he benefits from the upper part, because he needs a channel for the excess water; if it is blocked, water overflows onto his land and spoils his crop, and because each one benefits from the river from its beginning to its end. In Al-Khaniyya: the fatwa is on the saying of Imam. And our imams differed regarding a private path when it needs repair: it was said it is upon this difference: according to Imam, the cost is upon him until it passes his land; according to the two, from its beginning to...
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation of clearing (kari) rivers. For a non-owned (public) river, the cost is borne by the public treasury (bayt al-mal), specifically from jizya and kharaj funds, not from 'ushr or sadaqat. If the treasury is empty, the imam (ruler) forces the people to clear it, based on the precedent of Umar ibn al-Khattab. For a privately owned river, the obligation falls on the owners, and they can be forced to contribute. The text distinguishes between private and public rivers: if the river is exclusively beneficial to a specific group (i.e., it entitles them to shuf'a), it is private, and individuals are not forced to contribute; instead, the matter can be taken to a judge. For general rivers, the imam can force everyone. The central dispute concerns the apportionment of costs for clearing a shared river: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that each person is responsible only for the portion from the beginning up to his land; beyond his land, he is free. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that all users share the cost equally from top to bottom because everyone benefits from the entire river, including the outflow of excess water. The fatwa is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage also discusses a private path (tariq khas) analogously.

Key Terms
الكري (al-kari): The act of digging or clearing a river or canal to remove silt and maintain water flow.
الجزية والخراج (al-jizya wal-kharaj): Taxes collected from non-Muslims (jizya) and land tax (kharaj) used for public works like clearing rivers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa, how is the cost of clearing a shared river apportioned among the owners?
A) Equally among all owners from top to bottom.
B) Each owner pays only for the portion from the top to his own land.
C) The state pays entirely.
D) Only the owners at the lower end pay.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on clearing a shared river centres on whether the primary benefit is irrigation (Imam) or includes drainage (Sahibayn); the fatwa follows Imam's view, making downstream owners pay more.

Clearing a Non-Owned River from the Public Treasury
Ruling on maintenance cost for non-owned rivers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on funding the maintenance of public rivers and explain the conditions under which the imam may compel individuals.

Core Idea
Public river maintenance is from the treasury; if empty, the imam compels the able and rich to prevent greater harm.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on who bears the cost of clearing a river that is not privately owned. The default rule is that the state treasury (Bayt al-Mal) covers such public works, using funds from jizya and kharaj. However, when the treasury is empty, the imam has the authority to compel those who benefit from the river to contribute, based on the precedent of Caliph 'Umar. We will walk through the ruling, its evidence, and the limits of compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is clearing (kary) a river that is not owned (nahr ghayr mamlook). The default ruling is that the cost is wajib (obligatory) upon the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal).
This is because it serves the public interest, and Bayt al-Mal is designated for such needs.
2
Specify the funding source
According to al-Hidayah, the funds should come from jizya and kharaj, not from 'ushr or sadaqat, which are reserved for the poor.
This distinction ensures that charity funds reach their intended recipients.
3
Analyze the exception when treasury is empty
If there is no money in the treasury, the imam may compel the people (jabr) to clear the river. The reason is that leaving the river uncleared causes widespread harm (darar 'azim), and people rarely cooperate voluntarily.
Compulsion is a last resort to prevent greater harm.
4
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The proof is the action of Caliph 'Umar, who compelled people in a similar situation and said: 'If you left it, you would sell your children.' This indicates that compulsion is permissible to avoid ruinous harm.
The hadith is a practice of a Rightly-Guided Caliph, considered marfu' hukman (having the ruling of the Prophet).
5
Identify the limits of compulsion
Only those who are physically able to clear the river are required to work. For those who are unable (e.g., the wealthy who cannot do physical labor), they must bear the cost instead.
The imam cannot impose an unfair burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وكري نهر غير مملوك من بيت المال) لأن ذلك لمصلحة العامة وبيت المال معدلها . قال في الهداية : ويصرف ذلك من الجزية والخراج دون العشر والصدقات لأن الثاني للفقراء ولأول للنوائب. قال رحمه الله : (فإن لم يكن فيه شيء يجبر الناس على كربه يعني إذا لم يكن في بيت المال شيء أجبر الإمام الناس على كربه لأن الإمام نصب ناظراً وفي تركه ضرر عظيم على الناس وقلما يتفق العوام علي المصالح باختيارهم فيجبرهم عليه لما روي أن عمر أجبر في مثل هذا فكلموه فقال : لو تركتم لبعتم أولادكم إلا أنه يخرج للكري من كان يطيق الكري منهم ويجعل مؤته على الأغنياء الذي لا يطيقون الكري بأنفسهم. قال في الهداية : فإن أراد أن يحصص النهر خوف الانتشاف وفيه ضرر عظيم يجبرهم على ذلك اهـ
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And clearing a river that is not owned is from the public treasury) because that is for the public interest, and the public treasury is its place. He said in al-Hidayah: And that is spent from the jizya and kharaj, not from the 'ushr and sadaqat, because the second is for the poor and the first is for contingencies. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If there is nothing in it, people are compelled to clear it) meaning if there is nothing in the public treasury, the imam compels the people to clear it, because the imam is appointed as a supervisor, and leaving it causes great harm to the people, and the common people rarely agree voluntarily on those interests, so he compels them, based on what is narrated that 'Umar compelled in such a case, and they spoke to him, so he said: 'If you left it, you would sell your children.' Except that those who are able to clear among them are taken out for clearing, and its cost is placed on the rich who are unable to clear themselves. He said in al-Hidayah: If he wants to divide the river due to fear of seepage and there is great harm, he compels them to that. End.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on maintaining rivers that are not owned by anyone (public rivers). The initial ruling: the cost of clearing such a river is borne by the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). The commentator explains the reasoning: it is for public welfare, and the treasury is designated for such needs. He then quotes al-Hidayah specifying the source: the expenditure should come from jizya (poll tax) and kharaj (land tax) collections, not from 'ushr (tithe) or sadaqat (charity), because the latter are for the poor, while the former are for public contingencies. If the treasury is empty, the imam has the authority to compel the people who benefit from the river to clear it. This is because leaving it would cause widespread hardship, and people rarely cooperate voluntarily. The proof is the precedent of Caliph 'Umar, who compelled people in a similar situation, warning that otherwise they would end up selling their children due to ruin. However, the imam must only compel those who are physically able, and the cost is levied on the wealthy who cannot do the labor themselves. Additionally, if there is a risk of seepage causing great harm, the imam can compel partition of the river. This ruling emphasizes the state's role in public infrastructure and the principle that necessity justifies compulsion.

Key Terms
كري (kary): Clearing or dredging a river or canal of silt and obstructions.
نهر غير مملوك (nahr ghayr mamlook): A river that is not owned by any individual, i.e., public property.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From which source is the expenditure for clearing a non-owned river to be taken according to al-Hidayah?
A) 'Ushr and sadaqat
B) Jizya and kharaj
C) Zakat funds
D) Personal charity

Key Takeaway
Clearing a public river is a state responsibility; if the treasury is empty, the imam may compel the able to work and the rich to pay, based on 'Umar's precedent.

Clearing Owned Rivers: Public vs Private Enforcement
Ruling on clearing owned rivers with public/private distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between public and private rivers in Hanafi property law and apply the criterion of pre-emption rights to determine whether a refuser can be compelled.

Core Idea
Liability follows benefit: owners of a private river bear its clearing costs, but compulsion only applies for public harm.

In Hanafi water law, clearing an owned river is a maintenance obligation. But the method of enforcement depends on whether the river is public or private. This passage clarifies the distinction using the principle of pre-emption rights and the legal maxim 'al-ghurm bil-ghunm'. It also resolves an internal Hanafi dispute about compulsion.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on clearing owned rivers
The cost of clearing an owned river falls on its owners because they benefit exclusively. The default ruling is that if an owner refuses, he is compelled to contribute.
This is based on the maxim 'al-ghurm bil-ghunm' (liability accompanies gain).
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi view
There is a transmitted view (قيل) that if the river is private, the refuser is NOT compelled. This creates a dispute within the school.
The text supports the distinction between public and private.
3
Examine the criterion for public vs private
The key criterion is whether pre-emption (shuf'a) applies. If a river is such that pre-emption rights can be claimed, it is private (khaass); otherwise it is public (aamm).
Pre-emption is a right of specific owners, not a public utility.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) to partnership
The rule for private rivers is analogized to a partnership in cultivating land: if one partner refuses to spend, the other may spend with the judge's order and recover the share. Similarly, for a private river, the matter goes to the judge.
For public rivers, the judge cannot recover from many, so the imam compels directly.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text prefers the distinction (public vs private) and cites al-Muhit and the practice of Abu Ja'far. The mufta bihi is: for public rivers, compulsion; for private, recourse to judge.
The apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) also supports that private refusers are not compelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وكري ما هو مملوك على أهله ويجبر الآبي على كربه) لأنه منفعة لهم على الخصوص فتكون مؤنته عليهم ولأن الغرم بالغنم ومن أبى منهم يجبر، وقيل إن كان خاصاً لا يجبر والفاصل بين الخاص والعام أن ما يستحق به الشفعة خاص وما لا يستحق به الشفعة عام. وبيان الفرق أنه إذا كان عاماً فيه دفع ضرر عام فيجبر الآبي بخلاف الخاص، وفي الضرر الخاص يمكن الدفع بأن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي فينفق ويرجع على الممتنع بحصته، وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو جعفر، وصار كزرع بين شريكين امتنع من الانفاق فلصاحبه أن ينفق عليه بأمر القاضي ويرجع عليه بما أنفق فكذا هذا؛ كذا في المحيط بخلاف ما إذا كان عاماً لا يمكن الرجوع لكبرهم فيجبر الممتنع، ولا يقال في كراء النهر الخاص احياء له حقوق أهل الشفعة فيكون في تركه ضرر عام لأنا نقول : لا لأجل أهل الشفعة ألا ترى أن أهل النهر لو امتنعوا عن كربه لا يجبرهم في ظاهر الرواية لأنهم امتنعوا عن عمارة أراضيهم. ولو كان حق أهل الشفعة معتبراً لا جبر. التتارخانية : معناه أن ينقلوا نصيب الآبي من الشرب مقدار ما بيلغ قيمة ما أنفق . أحدهما جبر وفي
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And clearing what is owned is upon its owners, and the one who refuses is compelled to clear it) because it is a benefit specifically for them, so its cost is upon them, and because liability accompanies gain. Whoever refuses among them is compelled. And it is said: if it is private, he is not compelled. The distinction between private and public is that what entitles pre-emption is private, and what does not entitle pre-emption is public. Explanation of the difference: if it is public, it involves repelling a public harm, so the refuser is compelled, contrary to private. In private harm, one can raise the matter to the judge, who then spends and recovers the share from the refuser. The jurist Abu Ja'far adopted this, and it is like a crop between two partners when one refuses to spend; the other may spend by order of the judge and recover what he spent. So is this; as in al-Muhit. Contrary to when it is public, recovery is not possible due to their large number, so the refuser is compelled. And it cannot be said that in clearing a private river there is revival of the rights of pre-emption holders, so leaving it causes public harm, because we say: no, it is not for the sake of pre-emption holders. Do you not see that if the people of the river refuse to clear it, they are not compelled in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya), because they are refusing to cultivate their lands. If the right of pre-emption were considered, they would be compelled. al-Tatarkhaniyya: Its meaning is that they transfer the share of the refuser from the water right to the amount equal to the value of what was spent. One of them compulsion and in...
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on clearing an owned river (nahr mamlook). The general principle: the cost of maintenance falls on the owners because they benefit exclusively, and liability follows benefit (al-ghurm bil-ghunm). If an owner refuses, the central question is whether he can be compelled. The author notes a distinction: if the river is 'common' (عام) benefiting many, the refuser is compelled to prevent public harm. If the river is 'private' (خاص), for a specific few, the refuser is not compelled; instead, the matter can be taken to a judge who can order expenditure and recover the defaulter's share. This is analogized (qiyas) to a partnership in cultivation where one partner refuses to spend. The jurist Abu Ja'far adopted this view. The key criterion distinguishing public from private is whether the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) applies: if it does, the river is private; if not, it is public. Pre-emption is a right that applies to specific private property, not to public utilities. The text then refutes an objection: even if a private river affects the rights of pre-emption holders, that does not make it public because the pre-emption right is secondary. The proof from the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) is that if owners of a private river refuse, they are not compelled because they are merely neglecting their own land. If pre-emption rights were paramount, they would be compelled. Finally, al-Tatarkhaniyya suggests a solution: the refuser's water share is transferred equivalent to the expenses incurred by others.

Key Terms
خاص (khaass): Private; a river or canal benefiting a limited number of owners who have pre-emption rights.
عام (aamm): Public; a river benefiting many such that pre-emption does not apply.
الغرم بالغنم (al-ghurm bil-ghunm): Liability accompanies gain; whoever benefits must bear the costs.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Pre-emption; the right of a co-owner to purchase a share sold to an outsider.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the criterion to distinguish a private river from a public one according to this passage?
A) The number of owners
B) The width of the river
C) Whether pre-emption (shuf'a) is applicable
D) Whether the river is perennial

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi water law, the enforcement mechanism for clearing an owned river depends on its classification: public rivers require compulsion, private rivers require judicial recovery.

Cost Distribution for a Shared River: Top-to-Bottom Rule
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cost sharing for a shared river

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the allocation of clearing costs for a shared river, apply the Hanafi tarjih mechanism, and identify the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Each landowner pays only for the river segment benefiting his land; fatwa is on Imam Abu Hanifa's view.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding maintenance costs for a shared watercourse. The ruling affects farmers who irrigate sequentially from a common river. Understanding each side's reasoning—Imam's focus on direct benefit versus the Sahibayn's consideration of drainage needs—helps internalize Hanafi methodology and the process of tarjih within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
What is the legal problem addressed?
It is the allocation of clearing costs for a river shared by multiple landowners.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position
According to the Imam, each landowner bears cost only until the river passes his land; thereafter he is free (bari’).
This is the position of the text (قال رحمه الله ... وهذا عند الإمام).
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that all owners share the cost proportionally from top to bottom.
They argue downstream owners benefit from upstream drainage.
4
Analyse the Proofs
Imam relies on limited benefit and ability to block; Sahibayn rely on drainage necessity and overall use.
See hanafi_dalil and opposing_dalil in the madhhab_dispute_map.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Al-Khaniyya states the fatwa is on Imam's view. Also note the extension to private roads and the distinction by al-Hinduwani.
Practical application: upstream owners pay more under Imam; equal sharing under Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومؤنة كري النهر المشترك عليهم من أعلاه فإذا جاوز أرض رجل برئ) وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : المؤنة عليهم جميعاً من أول النهر إلى آخره بالحصص لأن كل واحد منهم ينتفع بالأسفل كما ينتفع بالأعلى لأنه يحتاج إلى مسيل الفاضل من الماء فإنه إذا سد عليه فاض الماء إلى أرضه فيفسد زارعه ولأن كل واحد منهم ينتفع بالنهر من أوله إلى أسفله. وفي الخانية : الفتوى على قول الإمام واختلف أئمتنا في الطريق الخاص إذا احتاج الإصلاح؛ قيل هو على هذا الاختلاف عند الإمام عليه المؤنة إلى أن يجاوز أرضه، وعندهما من أوله إلى آخره، قال الهندواني : ورأيت في بعض الكتب إذا انتهى إلى دار رجل يدفع عنه مؤنة الإصلاح بالإجماع فيحتاج إلى الفرق بين الطريق والنهر والفرق أن صاحب الدار لا يحتاج إلى النظر فيما جاوز داره بوجه من الوجوه بخلاف صاحب الأرض وللإمام أن مؤنة الكرب على من ينتفع به ويسقي منه أرضه فإذا جاوز أرضه برئ فلا يلزم شيء في مؤنة ما ألا بقي ترى أن من له الحق يسيل الماء على سطح جاره لا يلزمه شيء من عمارته باعتبار مسيل الماء فيه، ولأنه يتمكن من دفع الضرر عنه بسد فوة النهر من أعلاه إذا استغنى عنه. أن الكرب إذا انتهى إلى فوة أرضه من النهر فليس عليه شيء من المؤنة، والأصح أنه بعضهم يمكنه مؤنة الكرب إلى أن يجاوز حد أرضه لأن له أن يأخذ الفوة من أي موضع شاء أعلاها أو أسفلها.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the cost of clearing a shared river is upon them from its top; when it passes a man's land, he is free.) And this is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The cost is upon all of them from the beginning to the end in proportion, because each of them benefits from the lower part as he benefits from the upper, because he needs a passage for the surplus water; if it is blocked, the water overflows onto his land and spoils his crop. And because each of them benefits from the river from its beginning to its end. In al-Khaniyya: The fatwa is on the saying of the Imam. And our imams differed regarding a private road when it needs repair; it is said it follows this difference: according to the Imam, the cost is upon him until it passes his land; according to them, from beginning to end. Al-Hinduwani said: I saw in some books that when it reaches a man's house, the cost of repair is waived from him by consensus, so there is need to distinguish between a road and a river. The difference is that the owner of the house does not need to look beyond his house in any way, unlike the owner of land. According to the Imam, the cost of clearing is on the one who benefits and irrigates his land from it; when it passes his land, he is free, so nothing is due from him for the remaining cost. Do you not see that the one who has the right to let water flow onto the roof of his neighbor is not liable for anything for its maintenance due to the flow of water? And because he can repel the harm by blocking the mouth of the river from above when he is in need of it? That when clearing reaches the mouth of his land from the river, nothing of the cost is upon him. And the most correct is that some of them can bear the cost of clearing up to the limit of his land, because he can take the water from any place he wishes, either above or below.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the allocation of maintenance costs for a shared river. The issue: when clearing a river that serves multiple landowners sequentially (top to bottom), who bears the cost? Abu Hanifa's position: each landowner is liable only for the portion of the river from its start until it passes his land. Once the cleared section passes his land, his responsibility ends. The reasoning: his benefit is limited to the part that irrigates his land; downstream owners do not benefit from the upstream section once past. The two companions argue that all owners must share the cost in proportion from beginning to end, because even downstream owners benefit from the upper part for drainage of surplus water; if the upper part is blocked, water may back up and flood their land. They also note that everyone uses the entire river course. The fatwa (operative opinion) according to al-Khaniyya is on the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. The text then extends the discussion to a private road (tariq khaas) by analogy, with al-Hinduwani noting a consensus that a house owner is free from repair cost beyond his house, distinguishing between road and river based on need. The Imam's view is further supported by the example of a water drainage right on a neighbor's roof, where the right-holder is not liable for the roof's maintenance. Ultimately, the most correct view according to some is that the cost is borne up to the limit of one's land, as the owner can draw water from any point.

Key Terms
النهر المشترك (al-nahr al-mushtarak): A river shared by multiple landowners, typically used for irrigation sequentially.
من أعلاه (min a'lahu): From the top/upstream part of the river.
برئ (bari'a): Become free from liability for further cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa in al-Khaniyya, which view is operative regarding the cost of clearing a shared river?
A) The view of the two companions (Sahibayn)
B) The view of Imam Abu Hanifa
C) The view of al-Hinduwani
D) The view of consensus

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa for shared river clearing follows Imam Abu Hanifa: each landowner bears cost only until the river passes his land.
