Condition of Wounding (Jarh) in Hunting
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wounding condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether wounding is a condition for permissibility of hunting prey, and explain the evidence for each using usul al-fiqh principles.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school requires wounding for prey to be halal, though a weak minority view holds it unnecessary.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks an internal Hanafi difference on whether the hunting animal must actually wound the prey. The majority (zahir al-riwaya) insists on wounding to release blood, while a narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argues from the absolute wording of the Quran. The discussion introduces the crucial usul principle of carrying the absolute (mutlaq) on the qualified (muqayyad) when the ruling and incident are united.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the majority Hanafi position (wounding is required) and the minority view (wounding is not required) attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in a weak narration.
The minority is not the mufta bihi; it is from nawadir.
2
Examine the evidence for the minority
The minority relies on the absolute verse 'فكلوا مما أمسكن عليكم' (5:4) and a hadith from 'Adi and Tha'laba, which do not mention wounding. They argue that adding a condition is an impermissible addition to the text.
This is a straightforward appeal to the literal wording.
3
Examine the evidence for the majority
The majority responds that the absolute is carried on the qualified because the ruling and the incident are united. Additionally, the purpose is to release blood (as in slaughter), and without wounding the prey becomes like something beaten to death (موقوذه), which is prohibited by Quran 5:4 itself.
The principle of haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad is key.
4
Analyze the usul principle applied
The passage states that when the absolute and qualified come from the same cause or incident, the absolute is restricted. Here, the verses about hunting are considered one incident, so the general permission is qualified by the requirement of wounding.
This differs from cases where the incidents are separate.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The zahir al-riwaya (authoritative transmission) is that wounding is required, and this is the relied-upon position for fatwa. The minority view is a weak narration and not acted upon.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah similarly takes the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهر عبارة المؤلف الاكتفاء بالجرح سالماً أولاً لكن قال في المحيط : إن جرحه لم يدمه اختلفوا فيه قيل لا يحل وقيل يحل، وقيل إن كانت الجراحة صغيرة لا يحل إذا لم يرم وإن كانت كبيرة يحل . وأما الجرح فالمذكور هنا ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف أنه لا يشترط ؛ رواه الحسن عنهما وهو قول الشعبي لقوله تعالى : فكلوا مما أمسكن عليكم [المائدة : ٤] مطلقاً من غير قيد بالجرح فمن شرطه فقد زاد على النص وهو نسخ ما عرف في موضعه، وكذا ما روينا من حديث عدي وثعلبة يدل على ذلك لأنه مطلق فيجري على إطلاقه وإلا لزم نسخة بالرأي وهو لا يجوز الظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وما علمتم من الجوارح وهو يشير إلى ما قلنا ولأن المقصود إخراج الدم المسفوح وهو يخرج بالجرح عادة ولا يختلف عنه إلا نادراً فأقيم الجرح مقامه كما في الذكاة الاختيارية والرمي بالسهم، ولأنه إذا لم يجرحه صار موقوذه وهي محرمة بالنص وما تلي ،مطلق وكذا ما روي فحملناه على المفيد لاتحاد الواقعة. وإنما لم يحمل المطلق على المقيد فيما إذا اختلفت الحوادث أو كان التقييد والإطلاق من جهة. السبب، وأما إذا كان من جهة الحكم والحادثة واحدة فيحمل عليه.
The apparent wording of the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) is satisfaction with the wound initially without condition? But it is said in al-Muhit: If he wounded it but did not make it bleed, they differed: some said it is not lawful, some said it is lawful, and some said if the wound is small it is not lawful if it does not bleed, and if it is large it is lawful. As for the wound, what is mentioned here is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya). And from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it is not a condition; al-Hasan narrated from them, and it is the saying of al-Sha'bi, due to the saying of Allah: 'Eat of what they catch for you' (Quran 5:4) absolutely without the restriction of wound. So whoever makes it a condition has added to the text, and that is abrogation as known in its place. Similarly, what we narrated from the hadith of 'Adi and Tha'laba indicates that, because it is absolute (mutlaq) so it runs on its absoluteness, otherwise it would entail abrogation by opinion, which is not permissible. The apparent meaning of the verse: 'And what you have taught of hunting animals' (Quran 5:4) indicates what we said. And because the purpose is to let out the poured blood, and that generally comes out by wound, rarely differing, so the wound is made to stand in its place as in elective slaughter and shooting with an arrow. And because if it does not wound it, it becomes like something beaten to death, which is prohibited by the text. And what has been narrated, we carry it on the beneficial due to the unity of the incident. The absolute is not carried on the restricted when the incidents differ, or the restriction and absoluteness are from the side of the cause. But if it is from the side of the ruling and the incident is one, then it is carried upon it.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage discusses whether it is a condition that the hunting animal (dog, falcon, etc.) wound the prey and cause blood to flow. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq seems to require the wound, but the commentator notes that in al-Muhit there is a difference of opinion regarding the extent of the wound. The main point is the condition of wounding (jarh) itself. The 'zahir al-riwaya' (the authoritative transmission of the Hanafi school) requires that the hunting animal actually wounds the prey. However, there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (transmitted by Hasan ibn Ziyad) that wounding is not a condition; they argue from the general wording of the Quranic verse 'فكلوا مما أمسكن عليكم' which does not mention wounding, and from the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim and Tha'laba. The commentator then provides the proof for the requirement of wounding: the purpose is to release the blood, which normally happens through wounding, and if there is no wound, the prey becomes like a beaten animal (موقوذه) which is explicitly prohibited. The passage also explains the usul principle: if the absolute (mutlaq) and the qualified (muqayyad) arise from the same incident, the qualified restricts the absolute; but if they arise from different incidents, the absolute remains as is. Here, the absolute verses (like 'eat of what they catch') and the qualified (the condition of training and the verses about hunting animals) are considered as one incident, so the absolute is restricted by the condition of wounding.

Key Terms
الجرح (al-jarh): The act of wounding the prey by the hunting animal, causing blood to flow.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The authoritative and widely transmitted position within the Hanafi school, as opposed to rare narrations.
المطلق والمقيد (al-mutlaq wa al-muqayyad): Unqualified and qualified expressions in the primary texts; the principle of carrying the absolute on the qualified when they relate to the same ruling and incident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) regarding the need for wounding in hunting?
A) Wounding is not required.
B) Wounding is required only if the prey is large.
C) Wounding is required.
D) Wounding is haram.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that the hunting animal wound the prey to make it permissible to eat, based on the authoritative riwaya and the usul principle of carrying the absolute on the qualified.

Multiple Prey by One Irsal with One Tasmiyah
Ruling on multiple prey by one irsal with one tasmiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on multiple prey killed by one irsal with one tasmiyah and distinguish it from slaughter based on the unity of the causative act.

Core Idea
One tasmiyah at the time of sending suffices for all prey the animal kills.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key distinction between hunting and slaughter. In hunting, the act that renders the prey lawful is the sending (irsal). Therefore, a single tasmiyah at that moment covers all subsequent kills, even if the animal kills multiple prey one after another. The ruling is derived by analogy (qiyas) to shooting an arrow that hits multiple targets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act being ruled upon is sending a trained hunting animal (e.g., dog, falcon) with one tasmiyah, which then kills multiple prey.
The focus is on the sending (irsal), not each individual kill.
2
State the Ruling
All prey are lawful (halal) with that single tasmiyah, even if killed one after another.
The text explicitly says: 'ولو سمى حالة الإرسال فقتل الكل حلت'.
3
Recognize the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is qiyas to the case of shooting an arrow at one prey that passes through and hits another prey. In both cases, the causative act (shooting or sending) is one, and the tasmiyah is attached to that act.
The illa is the unity of the causative act.
4
Distinguish from Slaughter
In slaughter (dhabh), each animal requires a separate act of cutting, so a separate tasmiyah is needed, unless both are slaughtered together in one continuous act.
The text contrasts: 'بخلاف ما إذا ذبح شاتين بتسمية فإنه لا يحل'.
5
Confirm the Illa
The effective cause (illa) is that the act which causes permissibility (irsal) is single, and the tasmiyah is attached to that single act. Hence, one tasmiyah suffices for all results of that act.
This illa is jaliyyah (evident) and extracted via tahqiq al-manat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سمى حالة الإرسال فقتل الكل حلت ولو قتل الكل واحداً بعد واحد حل بخلاف ما إذا ذبح شاتين بتسمية فإنه لا يحل والفرق أن الحل في باب الصيد يحصل بالإرسال فتشترط التسمية وقت الإرسال والإرسال وجد وقت تسمية واحدة كما لو رمى سهماً إلى صيد فنفذ وأصاب صيداً آخر بخلاف ما لو ذبح شاة أخرى لأن الثانية صارت مذبوحة بفعل غير الأول فلا بد من تسمية أخرى، ولو اضجع شاتين وذبحها بتسمية واحدة حلا.
If he says the tasmiyah at the time of sending (the dog), and it kills all (the prey), they are lawful. And if it kills them one after another, they are lawful. Unlike if he slaughters two sheep with one tasmiyah, then it is not lawful. The difference is that permissibility in hunting is achieved by sending (the dog), so the tasmiyah is required at the time of sending, and the sending occurred at the time of one tasmiyah, just as if he shot an arrow at a prey and it passed through and hit another prey – it is lawful. Unlike if he slaughtered another sheep, because the second becomes slaughtered by an act other than the first, so another tasmiyah is required. And if he laid down two sheep and slaughtered them with one tasmiyah, they are lawful.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage differentiates between hunting and slaughter (dhabh) regarding the requirement of tasmiyah when multiple animals are involved. In hunting, the act that makes the prey lawful (hallal) is the sending (irsal) of the hunting animal (dog, bird of prey, etc.). Therefore, a single tasmiyah at the moment of sending suffices no matter how many prey the animal kills, even if killed one after another. This is analogous to shooting an arrow at one prey and it hitting another – both are lawful. In slaughter, the act that makes the animal lawful is the actual cutting of the throat. Thus, if one makes a single tasmiyah for two separate sheep, it is not sufficient because the second sheep is slaughtered by a separate act; a new tasmiyah is required for each animal. However, if two sheep are placed together and both are slaughtered with one continuous cut (same act), one tasmiyah suffices. The key factor is the unity of the causative act: in hunting, the sending is one act; in slaughter, each animal requires its own act unless they are slaughtered together in one act.

Key Terms
الإرسال (al-irsal): The act of sending a trained hunting animal (dog, falcon, etc.) after prey.
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' at the time of sending the hunting animal or at the time of slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter sends his dog with one tasmiyah and the dog kills two deer one after another, what is the ruling?
A) Only the first deer is lawful.
B) Only the second deer is lawful.
C) Both are lawful.
D) Neither is lawful.

Key Takeaway
One tasmiyah at the time of sending suffices for all prey killed by the hunting animal, because the causa permissiva is the irsal, not each kill.

Eating from the Prey: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Maliki
Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Maliki on ruling when hunting dog eats from the prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i/Maliki position on the ruling when a hunting dog eats from the prey, state the key evidence for each, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A dog eating from the prey indicates it caught for itself, making the meat unlawful – unlike a falcon.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the classic inter-madhhab dispute over the effect of a hunting dog eating from its catch. The Hanafi school rules that the prey becomes unlawful, while the Shafi'i and Maliki schools (in an old view) permit it. Understanding the evidence and reasoning behind each position is essential for applying the correct ruling in hunting scenarios.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school rules regarding a trained dog that eats from the prey.
The ruling is: the prey is not lawful (haram).
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Determine which schools disagree and what they rule instead.
Shafi'i and Maliki (old view) rule the prey is lawful even if the dog eats.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the evidence the opposing schools cite, particularly the hadith of 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba.
The hadith reportedly permits eating even if the dog eats.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi school rebuts the opposing evidence, including hadith criticism and the distinction between dogs and birds of prey.
The hadith of Tha'laba is considered weak; authentic hadiths and Quran 5:3 prohibit it.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference in rulings between the schools on this issue.
If a dog eats from the prey, Hanafis prohibit it; Shafi'is and Malikis (old) allow it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه بن عمر أن الله : (فإن أكل منه البازي أكل وإن أكل منه الكلب أو الفهد لا) وقال مالك والشافعي في القديم : يؤكل وإن أكل منه الكلب كالبازي لما روي عن عبد الله ثعلبة قال : يا رسول الله إن لي كلاباً مكلبة فأفتني في صيدها فقال : إن كانت لك كلاب مكلبة فكل ما أمسكت عليك الحديث إلى أن قال للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : وإن أكل منه؟ قال عليه الصلاة والسلام وإن أكل منه وفعل الكلب إنما صار ذكاة لعلمه وبالأكل لا فصار كالبازي. ولنا ما روينا من حديث عمر بن عدي وقوله تعالى: ﴿وما أكل السبع إلا ما ذكيتم [المائدة : ٣] وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إذا أرسلت كلابك المعلمة وذكرت اسم الله تعالى فكل ما أمسكن عليك إلا أن يأكل الكلب فلا تأكل فإني أخاف أن يكون إنما أمسك على نفسه رواه البخاري ومسلم وعن ابن عباس أنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم «إذا أرسلت كلبك المعلم فأكل من الصيد فلا تأكل وإنما أمسكه على نفسه وإذا أرسلته فقتل ولم يأكل فكل فإنما أمسك على صاحبه رواه أحمد ومرويهما غريب فلا يعارض الصحيح المشهور، فالمحرم أولى على ما عرف في موضعه والفرق بين البازي والكلب قد بيناه.
He (the author) said: 'If the falcon eats from it, it is eaten; but if the dog or cheetah eats from it, it is not.' Malik and al-Shafi'i in the old view said: it is eaten even if the dog eats from it, like the falcon, based on what was narrated from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba who said: O Messenger of Allah, I have trained dogs; give me a verdict regarding their catch. He said: 'If you have trained dogs, eat what they catch for you...' until he said to the Prophet: 'And if it eats from it?' He said: 'Even if it eats from it.' And the action of the dog becomes a (cause of) tadhkiyah because of its knowledge, and by eating it does not (negate that), so it becomes like the falcon. Our proof is what we narrated from the hadith of 'Umar ibn 'Adi and the saying of Allah: 'And what has been eaten by a wild animal except what you slaughter' (Quran 5:3), and his saying (peace be upon him): 'When you send your trained dogs and mention the name of Allah, eat what they catch for you, unless the dog eats, then do not eat, for I fear that it has caught for itself' – narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. And from Ibn 'Abbas that the Messenger of Allah said: 'When you send your trained dog and it eats from the prey, do not eat, for it has caught for itself; and when you send it and it kills without eating, then eat, for it has caught for its owner' – narrated by Ahmad, but their transmission is uncommon (gharib) and does not contradict the well-known authentic (hadith). So the prohibited is preferred, as known in its place. And the difference between the falcon and the dog we have already explained.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known inter-madhhab dispute regarding the effect of the hunting dog eating from the prey. The Hanafi position (and that of many scholars) is that if the dog eats from the prey, the meat is not lawful. This is based on the authentic hadith in Bukhari and Muslim: 'إذا أرسلت كلابك المعلمة... إلا أن يأكل الكلب فلا تأكل'. The Hanafis also cite the verse 'وما أكل السبع إلا ما ذكيتم' (Quran 5:3) which implies that if the animal eats from the prey, it is as if a wild animal ate it. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools, in an old view, held that the prey remains lawful even if the dog eats, citing a hadith from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba where the Prophet allowed it. The Hanafis respond that the hadith of Tha'laba is not as authentic or is abrogated, and the well-known hadiths from 'Umar ibn 'Adi and Ibn 'Abbas clearly prohibit eating from the catch if the dog ate, indicating that the dog caught for itself. Thus, the Hanafi position is based on stronger evidence and is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi). A key point is the distinction between dogs and birds of prey (like falcons): the falcon's eating does not indicate it caught for itself because its nature is to eat, but the dog's eating is a sign of self-interest. The Hanafis argue that the falcon's eating is out of instinct, not proof of lack of training, while the dog's eating shows it was not properly trained (mu'allam), as a trained dog should not eat from the catch without command.

Key Terms
البازي (al-bazi): A falcon or other bird of prey used for hunting.
الكلب المعلم (al-kalb al-mu'allam): A dog trained for hunting, which responds to command and does not eat the prey until the master gives permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a trained dog eats from the prey it caught?
A) The prey is lawful because the dog is trained.
B) The prey is not lawful because the dog caught for itself.
C) The prey is lawful only if the falcon also eats.
D) The ruling depends on the size of the prey.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits eating prey if the hunting dog eats from it, based on authentic hadith and the principle that the dog's eating indicates it caught for itself.

Effect of Dog Eating on Prior and Subsequent Prey: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on retroactive effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the permissibility of prey caught before a dog eats from another prey, and explain the rationale behind each position.

Core Idea
A dog's eating from prey indicates ignorance, but prior prey at home may remain halal if forgetfulness or hunger is plausible.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq examines a scenario where a dog catches multiple prey without eating, then later eats from one. The central intra-Hanafi dispute is whether this later eating retroactively invalidates the earlier catches. Imam Abu Hanifa held that it does, while his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argued that it does not, especially for prey already brought home. Understanding their reasoning is essential for applying the correct ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the triggering event
The dog catches several prey, eats none initially, then later eats from one. The question is whether earlier catches are affected.
2
Distinguish the ruling on the eaten prey and subsequent catches
All agree the prey from which the dog ate is haram, and subsequent catches are also haram until the dog is retrained.
This is based on the dog's eating being a sign of ignorance (jahl).
3
Analyze the dispute for earlier catches in the desert vs. at home
In the desert (mafaza), all agree earlier catches are haram. At home (muhriz), Abu Hanifa says haram; the Sahibayn say halal.
The difference hinges on whether the purpose of training has been achieved.
4
Examine the proofs of each position
Abu Hanifa: eating reveals the dog was never trained from the beginning. Sahibayn: eating may be due to forgetfulness or hunger; the earlier ijtihad is not overturned.
The Sahibayn also argue that the skill may be forgotten temporarily.
5
Apply the time-based refinement from later mashayikh
If a long period (e.g., one month) has passed, even Abu Hanifa's view does not apply due to plausible forgetfulness. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi confirms the dispute remains in both cases.
This refinement softens the practical outcome of Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو صاد الكلب صيوداً ولم يأكل منها شيئاً ثم أكل من صيد بعد ذلك لا يؤكل من الذي أكل منه لأن أكله علامة جهله ولا مما يصيده بعده حتى يصير معلماً على الاختلاف الذي بيناه في الابتداء. وأما الصيود التي أخذها من قبل فيما أكل منه لا تظهر الحرمة فيه لعدم المحلية وما ليس بمحرز بأن كان في المفازة بعد تثبت الحرمة بالاتفاق وما هو محرز في البيت يحرم عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وعندهما لا يحرم لأن الأكل لا يدل على جهله لأن الحرفة قد تنسى وقد يشتد عليه الجوع فيأكل مع علمه، ولأن ما أحرزه قد أمضى الحكم فيه الاجتهاد فلا يتنقض باجتهاد مثله لأن المقصود قد حصل بالأول بخلاف غير المحرز لأن المقصود لم يحصل فيه من كل وجه لبقاء الصيدية فيه من وجه لعدم الاحتراز فيحرم احتياطاً. ولأبي حنيفة رضي الله تعالى عنه أن أكله آية جهله من الابتداء لأن الحرفة لا ينسى أصلها فبالأكل تبين أن تركه الأكل كان بسبب الشبع لا للتعلم وقد تبدل الاجتهاد قبل حصول المقصود لأن المقصود يحصل بالأكل فصار كتبدل اجتهاد القاضي قبل القضاء، ولأن علمه لا يثبت إلا ظاهراً فبقي جهله موهوماً والموهوم في باب الصيد يلحق بالمتحقق احتياطاً ما أمكن والإمكان في حق القائم جميعاً دون الفائت. قال بعض المشايخ: إنما تحرم تلك الصيود عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى إذا كان العهد قريباً، أما إذا تطاول العهد بأن أتى عليه شهراً وأكثر وصاحبه قدر تلك الصيود لا نحرم تلك الصيود في قولهم جميعاً لأن المدة الطويلة يتحقق النسيان فلا يعلم أنه لم يكن معلماً في الماضي من الزمان، وفي المدة القصيرة لا يتحقق النسيان فيظهر أنه لم يكن معلماً حين اصطياد تلك الصيود فتحرم تلك الصيود. وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : الصحيح أن الخلاف في الفصلين.
If the dog caught several prey and did not eat from any of them, then later ate from one prey, that prey from which he ate is not eaten because his eating is a sign of his ignorance (lack of training). And neither are (the prey) that he catches after it, until he becomes trained, according to the difference we mentioned at the beginning. As for the prey that he took before (he ate), the prohibition does not appear in them due to lack of (remaining) prey status (mahalliya). And what is not enclosed (in the home) i.e., if it is in the desert, the prohibition is established by agreement. And what is enclosed in the house is forbidden according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not forbidden, because eating does not indicate his ignorance, for the skill may be forgotten and extreme hunger may overcome him so he eats despite his knowledge, and because what he has caught (and brought home) the ruling has been passed upon it by ijtihad, so it is not overturned by a similar ijtihad, because the purpose has been achieved with the first (capture). Unlike what is not enclosed, because the purpose has not been achieved completely due to the prey-status remaining partially due to lack of enclosure, so it is forbidden as a precaution. And for Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him: his eating is a sign of his ignorance from the beginning, because the skill is not forgotten in its origin. So by eating, it becomes clear that his leaving off eating was due to satiety, not learning. And ijtihad may change before the purpose is achieved, because the purpose is achieved by eating (?) Actually the text: 'لأن المقصود يحصل بالأكل' might be a typo; it should be 'بالإمساك'. The purpose is achieved by catching. His ijtihad changed like the change of a judge's ijtihad before passing judgment. And because his knowledge does not become established except apparently, so his ignorance remains hypothetical, and the hypothetical in the chapter of hunting is attached to the actual as a precaution insofar as possible, and possibility is in respect of what (the prey) that is still present all together, not the lost (already consumed). Some mashayikh said: The prohibition of those prey according to Abu Hanifa only applies if the time is recent. But if the time is long, like a month or more, and the owner is aware of those prey, we do not forbid those prey in all their views, because a long period establishes the possibility of forgetfulness, so it is not known that he was not trained in the past time. In a short period, forgetfulness is not established, so it appears that he was not trained at the time of hunting those prey, so those prey are forbidden. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The correct view is that the difference (between Abu Hanifa and the two) applies to both cases (recent and distant time).
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a dog has caught multiple prey without eating any, then later eats from one of the prey. The ruling is that the specific prey from which it ate is not permissible because the eating indicates the dog was not properly trained (ignorance). Furthermore, any prey caught after this incident is also not permissible until the dog becomes trained again. The more complex issue is regarding the prey that the dog caught before it ate (i.e., the earlier catches). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that those earlier catches are also forbidden if they are brought home (an enclosed place), because the dog's later eating retroactively shows that it was never truly trained, so all its catches are invalid. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the earlier catches remain permissible because they were caught when the dog was still presumed trained, and the later eating could be due to forgetfulness or extreme hunger, not necessarily lack of training. They also distinguish between prey that is still in the desert (not yet brought home) and prey that is already at home: for prey in the desert, all agree they become forbidden (because the purpose of training is not yet fully achieved), but for prey at home, only Abu Hanifa declares them forbidden. The passage also mentions a refinement from some later scholars (mashayikh) regarding the time factor: if a significant period (e.g., a month) has passed since the earlier catches, even Abu Hanifa's view is not applied because forgetfulness becomes plausible. Finally, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi states that the difference of opinion remains in both cases (recent and distant). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is typically the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) when the prey are already brought home, unless there is clear evidence of the dog's ignorance from the beginning.

Key Terms
المحرز (al-muhriz): Prey that has been brought home or to a secure place, considered already in the possession of the hunter.
المفازة (al-mafaza): Desert or open land where the prey is not yet brought home.
جهله (jahluhu): The dog's ignorance or lack of proper training (not being mu'allam).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on prey that the dog caught before it ate from another prey, if that prey is already at home (muhriz)?
A) They are haram because the eating shows the dog was never trained.
B) They are halal because the dog may have forgotten or been hungry.
C) They are halal only if the dog was trained before.
D) They are haram only if the dog ate from them.

Key Takeaway
The dog's later eating from prey does not automatically prohibit earlier catches when brought home; the Sahibayn's view is relied upon, but time and location affect the ruling.

Ruling on a Falcon that Returns After Fleeing
Expansion of the dog's ruling to the falcon via analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the catch of a falcon that returns after being lost and explain the legal analogy to a dog that eats from the prey.

Core Idea
A falcon that flees and returns becomes untrained; its catch is forbidden until retrained.

The Hanafi school extends a previous ruling about a dog that eats from its prey to a new case: a falcon that flies away and stays away for a while. The key is training – if the falcon loses its habituation to the owner, its hunting is no longer valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the condition for permissible hunting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage gives a specific scenario: a falcon flees, stays away for a while, returns, and is sent to hunt. The ruling is that its catch is not eaten (not permissible).
The ruling is on the catch, not on the falcon itself.
2
Understand the reason
The falcon is judged to be 'ignorant' (untrained) because it left the state of being trained (mu'allam) by its prolonged absence. Training requires habituation and obedience, which is broken by the absence.
The Arabic uses 'جهل' (ignorance) to mean untrained, not a moral state.
3
Trace the analogy to the dog
The passage explicitly says the falcon's ruling is like that of a dog when it eats from the prey. For a dog, eating from the prey indicates it is not properly trained, so its catch is not permissible.
The shared illa (effective cause) is the loss of training – for the dog it is eating, for the falcon it is flight.
4
Apply the analogy to the falcon
Since the falcon's flight breaks training, it is treated as untrained. Therefore, its catch is impermissible, just as an untrained dog's catch is impermissible.
The analogy is qiyas – the asl (dog), far' (falcon), illa (loss of training), and hukm (impermissibility of catch).
5
Note the condition for permissibility
Implicitly, the catch would be permissible if the falcon had not fled and remained trained. The passage indicates that the state of training must be continuous.
Retraining would restore permissibility, though not mentioned here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أن صقراً فر صاحبه فمكث حيناً ثم رجع إلى صاحبه فأرسله فصاد لا يؤكل صيده لأنه ترك ما صار به معلماً فيحكم بجهله كالكلب إذا أكل من الصيد فيبقى حكمه كحكم الكلب فيما ذكرنا.
If a falcon of a person flees and stays away for a while, then returns to its owner and he sends it and it hunts, its catch is not eaten because it left (the state of) being trained, so it is judged to be ignorant (untrained), like the dog when it eats from the prey, so its ruling remains like the ruling of the dog in what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This short passage extends the ruling of the dog that eats from the prey to a falcon that flies away and is lost for a period. The rationale is that the falcon, by leaving its owner and staying away, has broken the state of being trained (mu'allam). Training involves habituation to the owner and responding to his call. If the falcon leaves and stays away for some time, it loses that habituation and becomes wild again (ignorant). Therefore, when it returns and is sent for hunting, its catch is not permissible, even if it does not eat from the prey. This is analogous to the dog that eats from the prey: the eating is a sign of ignorance, and likewise the falcon's flight indicates ignorance. Thus, the falcon's catch is treated like that of an untrained dog: not permissible until it is retrained.

Key Terms
الصقر (al-saqr): A falcon or bird of prey used for hunting.
المعلم (al-mu'allam): Trained (hunting animal) that responds to the owner's command and does not eat from the prey without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason the falcon's catch is not eaten after it returns?
A) Because the falcon was wild by nature and cannot be trained.
B) Because it left the state of being trained and is judged ignorant.
C) Because the owner forgot to retrain it.
D) Because the falcon ate from the prey.

Key Takeaway
A falcon that flees and stays away for a while loses its trained status; its catch is impermissible until retrained.

Exceptions to Dog's Consumption Invalidating Prey
Rulings on exceptions to dog's consumption invalidating prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions where a dog's consumption does not invalidate the prey and explain the underlying principle of saydiyya.

Core Idea
A dog drinking blood or eating from a piece given after delivery does not invalidate the prey.

When hunting with a trained dog, a common concern is whether the dog's consumption of some part of the prey renders the rest impermissible. The Hanafi school provides clear exceptions: drinking blood and eating from a piece voluntarily given by the hunter after taking possession. This lesson dissects the rationale and the crucial concept of 'saydiyya' (prey status).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two exceptions
From the passage, locate the two scenarios where the dog's consumption does not invalidate the prey: (1) drinking blood only, (2) eating a piece that the hunter himself cuts and throws to the dog after taking possession.
2
Understand the principle of saydiyya
The key distinction is whether the consumption occurs before or after the hunter has secured the prey (ihraz). If the dog eats while the prey is still in the state of 'saydiyya' (i.e., not yet owned), it invalidates. If it eats after the hunter has taken possession, the prey is no longer considered 'prey' for that purpose.
Drinking blood is never considered eating, so it does not break the condition even before ihraz.
3
Note the condition for prohibition
The general rule is that eating from the prey before ihraz renders it haram. The exceptions are specific: blood drinking (not eating) and eating from a piece given after delivery (not eating from the prey itself).
The dog snatching a piece after delivery is treated the same as being given.
4
Apply the distinction
If a dog bites the prey and cuts a piece before the hunter secures it, the prey is not halal. This contrasts with the exceptions. The passage promises further explanation that is cut off.
The saydiyya remains until actual possession (ihraz) by the hunter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شرب الكلب من دم الصيد ولم يأكل من لحمه شيئاً أكل لأنه ممسك عليه، وهذا من غاية علمه حيث شرب ما لا يصلح لصاحبه وأمسك عليه ما يصلح له ولو أخذ الصائد الصيد من الكلب وقطع له منه قطعة وألقاها إليه فأكلها يؤكل ما بقي لأنه أمسك على صاحبه وسلمه إليه وأكله بعد ذلك مما ألقى إليه صاحبه لا يضره لأنه لم يأكل من الصيد وهو عادة الصيادين فصار كما إذا ألقى إليه طعاماً آخر. وكذا إذا خطف الكلب منه وأكله لأنه لم يأكل من الصيد إذ لم يبق صيداً في هذه الحالة والشرط ترك الأكل من الصيد وقد وجد فصار كما إذا افترس شاة بخلاف ما إذا فعل ذلك قبل أن يحرزه المالك لبقاء جهة الصيدية وسيأتي الفرق فيه ولو نهش الصيد فقطع منه من
If the dog drinks from the blood of the prey and does not eat any of its meat, it is eaten (the prey is lawful) because it caught for its owner. This is part of its extreme knowledge (training) because it drank what is not suitable for its owner and caught for him what is suitable. If the hunter takes the prey from the dog, cuts off a piece for it and throws it to it, and it eats it, what remains is eaten (lawful) because it caught for its owner and handed it over to him, and its eating after that from what its owner threw to it does not harm (the permissibility), because it did not eat from the prey (as it was no longer prey at that point), and this is the habit of hunters. So it is as if he threw other food to it. Similarly, if the dog snatches it from him and eats it, (it is permissible) because it did not eat from the prey since it is no longer prey in this situation, and the condition (of not eating from the prey) is observed (since the dog took from the hunter's hand after delivery). It becomes like if it (the dog) killed a goat (and ate it, that does not affect previous catches). This is different from if it did that (ate) before the owner had secured the prey, because the prey aspect (saydiyya) remains, and the difference will come later. And if it (the dog) bit the prey and cut off from it... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage provides two specific scenarios where the dog's consumption of something from the prey does not make the prey unlawful. First, if the dog drinks the blood of the prey but does not eat the meat, the prey is lawful. The reasoning is that drinking blood is actually a sign of the dog's good training: it knows that what is good for its owner (the meat) should be preserved, while the blood is of no use to the owner. This shows the dog caught for its owner. Second, if the hunter himself cuts a piece of the prey and gives it to the dog, and the dog eats it, the rest of the prey is lawful. This is because the dog has already surrendered the prey to the owner, and the piece given is like any other food; the dog is not eating from the prey while it is still in its possession as prey. Similarly, if the dog snatches a piece from the hunter after the prey has been delivered, it is considered the same. The key condition for prohibition is that the dog eats from the prey while the prey is still in a state of 'saydiyya' (being prey, i.e., before the hunter has taken possession and secured it). The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the case where the dog bites the prey and cuts a piece before the owner secures it, which will cause prohibition, and promises to explain the difference later.

Key Terms
الدم المسفوح (al-damm al-masfuh): The blood that flows from the prey; not suitable for human consumption.
الصيدية (al-saydiyya): The quality of being considered prey (i.e., something to be hunted and not yet owned).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter gives a piece of the prey to the dog after taking it from the dog, and the dog eats it, what is the ruling on the remaining meat?
A) The remaining meat is not lawful because the dog ate from it.
B) The remaining meat is lawful because the dog ate from a piece already separated by the owner.
C) The remaining meat is lawful only if the piece was small.
D) The entire prey is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A well-trained dog's drinking blood or eating from a piece given by the hunter after delivery does not affect the permissibility of the prey, provided the general condition of not eating before ihraz is observed.

Hunted Animal Found Alive: The Obligation of Slaughter
Explains the condition of a hunted animal's life and the requirement of slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a hunted animal found alive and apply the dalil from Quran and Sunnah to justify the requirement of slaughter.

Core Idea
If you find hunted game alive, slaughter it — the proof is absolute and no excuse exempts you.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi ruling on what happens when a hunter finds his prey alive after a trained dog or arrow has struck it. The key question is whether slaughter is still required or whether the wound suffices. The text uses absolute Quranic and prophetic proofs to establish that slaughter is mandatory, and it responds to a detailed distinction made by Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding this ruling clarifies a common point of confusion in the fiqh of hunting.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the situation: a hunter's trained dog or arrow catches game, and the hunter finds it alive. What is the legal status of the animal?
2
Identify the ruling
From the passage, determine the hukm: is slaughter wajib or merely recommended? What is the consequence of not slaughtering?
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that slaughter is wajib, and eating without it is not permitted.
3
Locate the dalil
Find the two proofs cited: Quran 5:3 and the hadith of Adi ibn Hatim. Note their absolute wording.
The Quran makes an absolute exception for slaughtered animals; the hadith commands slaughter with an imperative.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how each proof indicates wujub: the imperative 'fadhbah' in the hadith and the absolute exception in the verse.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-wujub, and al-istithna' al-mutlaq yufidu al-'umum.
5
Note the ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i
State al-Shafi'i's distinction between inability due to tool loss and time constraint, and the Hanafi response that the proofs are absolute and reject any distinction.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences for the hunter's negligence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوسف رحمه الله تعالى أن تكون بحال يعيش مثلها فيكون موتها مضافاً إلى الذكاة والسهم مثله. وإن كان فيه من الحياة فوق ما يكون في المذبوح فكذلك في رواية عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله تعالى وهو قول الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى لأنه يقدر الأصل فصار كالمتيمم إذا رأى الماء ولم يقدر على استعماله، ولا يؤكل في ظاهر الرواية لأنه قادر حكماً لثبوت يده عليه وهو قائم مقام التمكن من الذبح إذ لا يمكن اعتبار الذبح بعينه حقيقة لأن الناس يختلفون فيه على حسب تفاوتهم في الكياسة والهداية في أمر الذبح ولا يمكن ضبطه فأدير الحكم على ثبوت اليد لأنه هو المشاهد المعاين فلا يحل الأكل إلا بالذكاة، سواء كانت حياته خفية أو بينة لجرح المعلم أو غيره من السباع وعليه الفتوى لقوله تعالى: ﴿وما أكل السبع إلا ما ذكيتم ﴾ [المائدة: ٣] استثناه مطلقاً من غير تفصيل فيتناول كل حي مطلقاً . وكذا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لعدي فإذا أمسك عليك فأدركته حياً فاذبحه» مطلق مطلقاً، والحديث صحيح رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد. وفصل الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى تفصيلاً آخر غير ما ذكرنا فقال : إن لم يتمكن من الذبح لفقد الآلة لم يؤكل لأن التقصير من جهته، وإن كان لضيق الوقت أكل لعدم التقصير، والحجة عليه ما تلونا وما روينا.
Yusuf (Abu Yusuf) – may Allah have mercy on him – that it should be in a state such that a similar [animal] would live, so its death is attributed to the slaughter [tazkiyah], and the arrow is like that. If it has life more than what is in a slaughtered animal, then similarly, in a narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on them – and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i – may Allah have mercy on him – because he is capable of the origin [slaughter], so he becomes like the one who performs tayammum when he sees water but is unable to use it. But it is not eaten in the apparent narration because he is considered capable by virtue of his hand being upon it, and it stands in place of ability to slaughter, since it is not possible to consider actual slaughter in reality because people differ in it according to their varying skill and guidance in the matter of slaughter, and it cannot be standardized. So the ruling is made dependent on the presence of the hand because it is what is witnessed and observed. So eating is not permitted except with tazkiyah, whether its life is hidden or apparent, due to the wound of the trained dog or other beasts. And upon it is the fatwa, due to the saying of the Most High: 'And what a beast of prey has eaten except that which you slaughtered' [5:3] – He made an exception absolutely without detailing, so it includes every living creature absolutely. And likewise his saying – peace be upon him – to Adi: 'So if it catches for you and you find it alive, then slaughter it' – absolute, absolutely. The hadith is authentic, narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. And al-Shafi'i – may Allah have mercy on him – made another detail other than what we mentioned: he said: If he is unable to slaughter due to lack of a tool, it is not eaten because the shortcoming is from his side; but if due to lack of time, it is eaten because there is no shortcoming. The proof against him is what we have recited and what we have narrated.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and a brief inter-madhhab dispute. It opens by stating the general principle: when a trained dog or arrow hits game and the hunter finds it alive, its permissibility hinges on whether the hunter can slaughter it. The text first narrates from Abu Yusuf that the animal should be in a condition where it could live if left, meaning it has enough vital force. In such a case, its death is attributed to the hunter's deliberate act of slaughter (if performed) or to the arrow (if death occurs without slaughter but the hunter was unable to slaughter). The ruling mentions a narration from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that if the animal has life exceeding that of a normally slaughtered animal (i.e., it is very lively), the same rule applies: it requires slaughter. This is also the view of al-Shafi'i in one opinion. The reasoning is that the hunter has capacity over the animal (because it is in his possession), so he is obligated to slaughter it, analogous to a person performing tayammum who sees water but cannot use it. However, the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) states that it is not eaten unless slaughtered, because the hunter is legally considered capable of slaughter due to his control over the animal. The relied-upon fatwa is that slaughter is required whether the animal's life signs are faint or strong, based on the Quranic verse (5:3) and the hadith to Adi ibn Hatim that command slaughter upon finding the animal alive. The passage then records a difference with Imam al-Shafi'i, who distinguishes between inability due to lack of tools (not eaten) and inability due to time constraint (eaten). The Hanafi response is that the general proofs (Quran and hadith) do not make this distinction, so it is rejected.

Key Terms
ذكاة (tazkiyah): Islamic ritual slaughter of an animal by cutting the throat, making it permissible to eat.
جرح المعلم (jarh al-mu'allam): The wound inflicted by a trained (hunting) dog, which is considered a valid means of making the game halal if the dog is trained and sent with tasmiyah.
حياة خفية (hayyat khafiyyah): Life that is hidden or barely perceptible, such as slight movement of a limb after death-like state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter sends his trained dog, which catches a deer. The hunter finds the deer alive but with a faint pulse. According to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa in this passage, what must the hunter do?
A) He may eat it without slaughter because the dog is trained.
B) He must slaughter it before eating.
C) He must release it because it is still alive.
D) He must wait until it dies naturally.

Key Takeaway
A hunted animal found alive requires ritual slaughter regardless of the state of its life signs or the hunter's ability to slaughter.

Strangling (khanq) by a Dog: Why It Does Not Make the Prey Halal
Explains invalidity of killing by strangulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a dog kills by strangulation and identify the missing condition that renders the prey invalid.

Core Idea
Strangling prevents blood flow, so the required wound (jurh) is absent; thus the animal is not halal.

In hunting, a dog's killing of the prey must involve a wound that draws blood. If the dog strangles the animal by compressing its throat without making an open wound, the animal is not considered properly slaughtered. This lesson explains the rationale and the parallel ruling for breaking bones without an open wound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of strangling (khanq)
Recognize that strangulation refers to killing by squeezing the throat without cutting or piercing the skin. The text contrasts this with a wound that causes blood to flow.
2
Recall the condition for halal hunting
The dog must be trained, the hunter must say 'Bismillah' (tasmiyah), and the dog must cause a wound (jurh) that allows blood to escape.
These three conditions are established in earlier sections.
3
Apply the ruling to strangulation and bone-breaking
Since strangulation and breaking bones (kasar) do not produce an open wound that lets blood flow, they fail the wounding condition. Therefore the prey is not halal.
The text mentions a difference of narration about bone-breaking, but the ruling is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا خنقه الكلب ولم يجرحه فلما بينا عند قوله لا بد من التعليم والتسمية والجرح، وذكرنا اختلاف الرواية والكسر كالخنق حتى لا يعتد به لأنه لا يفضي إلى خروج الدم. وأما إذا شاركه كلب غير معلم أو كلب مجوسي أو كلب لم يذكر اسم الله تعالى عليه عمداً فلما روينا عن عدي بن حاتم رضي الله تعالى عنه أنه قال قلت: يا رسول الله إني أرسل كلبي فأسمي قال : إذا أرسلت كلبك وسميت فأخذ فقتل فكل، لا تأكل فإن منه فلا تأكل فإنما أمسك على نفسه قلت : إني أرسل كلبي فأجد معه كلباً آخر غيره كلبك كلباً لا أدري أيهما أخذ فقال : لا تأكل فإنما سميت على كلبك. فإن وجدت مع غيره وقد قتل فلاناً كل لأنك لا تدري أيهما قتله. رواهما البخاري ومسلم وأحمد رحمهم الله تعالى. وهذا صحيح فيكون حجة على مالك والشافعي في قوله القديم لأنه لا يحرم بأكل الكلب الصيد وعلى الشافعي في متروك التسمية عمداً أيضاً ولأنه اجتمع فيه المبيح والمحرم فيغلب فيه جهة الحرمة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ما اجتمع الحلال والحرام إلا وقد غلب الحرام الحلال وإن الحرام واجب الترك والحلال جائز الترك فكان الاحتياط في الترك.
And as for when the dog strangles him and does not wound him – based on what we explained at his saying: 'There must be training, naming (tasmiyah), and wounding.' And we mentioned the difference of narration, and breaking (the bone) is like strangling in that it is not considered, because it does not lead to the exit of blood. And as for when an untrained dog, or a dog of a Magian, or a dog upon which the name of Allah was intentionally not mentioned, participates – based on what we narrated from Adi ibn Hatim, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: I said, O Messenger of Allah, I send my dog and I say the name (of Allah). He said: 'When you send your dog and you have named, and it catches and kills, then eat. If it eats from it, then do not eat, for it caught for itself.' I said: I send my dog and I find with it another dog – I do not know which of them caught. He said: 'Do not eat, for you named over your dog.' If you find with another and it has killed someone – eat, because you do not know which killed it. Both are narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad, may Allah have mercy on them. This is authentic, and it is a proof against Malik and al-Shafi'i in his old saying, because he does not prohibit due to the dog eating the catch, and also against al-Shafi'i regarding the one who intentionally omits tasmiyah. And because in it the permissive and prohibitive causes are combined, so the aspect of prohibition prevails, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Halal and haram never meet but that haram overcomes halal.' And indeed haram is obligatory to leave, while halal is permissible to leave, so caution lies in leaving.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage deals with three sub-rulings regarding hunting with dogs. First, if the dog strangles the prey without wounding it (i.e., kills by crushing the throat without making blood flow), the animal is not halal. This is because the primary requirement for making game halal through a dog is that the dog wounds it and causes blood to flow; strangulation does not meet this condition. The same applies if the dog breaks the bone without an open wound. Second, if an untrained dog, a dog owned by a Magian (Zoroastrian), or a dog sent without intentionally saying 'Bismillah' participates in the catch, the animal becomes haram even if the hunter's own trained dog was also involved. The proof is the well-known hadith of Adi ibn Hatim, in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructed Adi not to eat if another dog (whose sending was not accompanied by tasmiyah) was present with his own dog, because he only named over his dog. The hadith also indicates that if the dog eats from the prey, it is not to be eaten, as that shows the dog caught for itself. The passage notes that this authentic hadith is evidence against Imam Malik (who does not consider the dog eating the prey as prohibitive) and against Imam al-Shafi'i in his old opinion (who also did not prohibit due to the dog eating). The general principle is that when halal and haram factors combine, the haram side prevails, as per the prophetic saying: 'Halal and haram never meet but that haram overcomes halal.' Therefore, caution dictates leaving the prey.

Key Terms
خنق (khanq): Strangling; killing by compressing the throat without cutting or piercing.
كسر (kasar): Breaking bones without an open wound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a prey animal killed by a dog's strangulation?
A) Halal, because the dog is trained.
B) Halal, only if tasmiyah was said.
C) Not halal, because no wound caused blood flow.
D) Not halal, regardless of conditions.

Key Takeaway
Strangling or breaking bones without an open wound fails the required wound condition, so the prey is not halal.

Participation of an Untrained Dog or a Dog Sent Without Tasmiyah
Explains prohibition when untrained dog or dog without tasmiyah joins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that when an untrained dog, a Magian's dog, or a dog sent without tasmiyah participates in the catch, the prey becomes haram, citing the hadith of Adi ibn Hatim.

Core Idea
If an unqualified dog joins the hunt, the prey is haram because the hunter only named over his own dog.

When a hunter sends his trained dog, but another dog that is untrained, belongs to a Magian, or was sent without saying 'Bismillah' joins and helps catch the prey, the animal becomes forbidden. This ruling is based on the explicit hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade eating when another dog was present. This lesson examines the three categories and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of disqualifying participants
The text mentions: an untrained dog (ghayr mu'allam), a dog of a Magian (Majuusi), and a dog upon which Allah's name was intentionally not mentioned (lam yudhkar ism Allah 'alayhi 'amdan).
These are considered unqualified because they lack proper training, ownership by a non-Muslim who does not follow Islamic slaughter rules, or lack of tasmiyah.
2
Locate the hadith of Adi ibn Hatim
The author quotes the hadith from Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad: Adi asks about sending his dog and finding another dog with it; the Prophet replied, 'Do not eat, for you named over your dog and not over the other.'
The hadith also includes a variant about the dog eating from the prey.
3
Understand the prohibition
Because the hunter only named over his own dog, the participation of an unqualified dog introduces uncertainty about which dog actually killed the prey. The prohibition is clear.
The hadith uses the phrase 'la ta'kul' (do not eat), indicating haram.
4
Apply the ruling to the three categories
The same prohibition applies whether the other dog is untrained, a Magian's dog, or one sent without tasmiyah. In all cases, the prey is haram.

The Principle That Haram Prevails Over Halal (Ghalaba al-Haram)
Presents the principle that haram prevails over halal to support the ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that when halal and haram factors combine, haram prevails, and use it to justify the Hanafi position in the inter-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
When permissive and prohibitive causes meet, the prohibition side overpowers, so caution dictates leaving the prey.

The author concludes the passage by stating that the Hanafi position is supported by a general principle: when halal and haram factors combine, haram overcomes halal. This principle, attributed to the Prophet (peace be upon him), justifies the ruling that the prey is not eaten when doubt arises from participation of unqualified dogs. This lesson explores the principle and its application in the dispute with Malik and Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The text cites the prophetic saying: 'Halal and haram never meet but that haram overcomes halal.' This is a maxim of caution.
The author uses this to strengthen the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the dispute context
The Hanafi position that prey is haram when a dog eats from it or when an unqualified dog participates is challenged by Malik and Shafi'i (old) who see permissive causes (the trained dog, tasmiyah) as sufficient.
The Hanafi says the prohibitive causes (eating, participation) outweigh the permissive.
3
Apply the principle to the ruling
In cases of doubt, the principle demands prohibition: 'haram is obligatory to leave, halal is permissible to leave.' So caution is to leave the prey.
This principle is a qa'ida in Hanafi legal theory.
4
Conclude the tarjih
Thus, the Hanafi ruling is preferred because it aligns with caution and the general principle.

Source Text

Ruling When a Second Dog Returns the Prey
Explains the effect of a second dog returning the prey without wounding it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two views (karahah tanzih and karahah tahrimee) regarding a second dog's involvement in hunting and apply the correct Hanafi ruling to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
If an untrained dog only returns the prey without wounding, the meat is disliked but not haram — though some Hanafi scholars consider it strongly prohibited.

In Hanafi fiqh, hunting with trained dogs is permitted under strict conditions. But what happens when an untrained dog assists by returning the prey to the hunter? This lesson examines the nuanced ruling, highlighting an internal Hanafi dispute on whether the dislike is light (tanzih) or severe (tahrimee).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
Read the passage to understand the specific situation: a trained dog wounds the prey, a second dog returns it without wounding, and the prey dies from the first wound. The ruling is that the meat is makruh (disliked).
Note that the second dog is not necessarily untrained; it is simply a dog that did not participate in the wounding.
2
Understand the basis for karahah
The karahah arises because the second dog assisted in the catching (taking or returning) but did not assist in the wounding. This partial involvement creates legal dislike.
The key distinction is between assistance in catching vs. wounding.
3
Analyze the two positions on the severity of karahah
The passage presents two views: (1) karahah tanzih – the meat is halal but disliked, because the first dog alone caused death; (2) karahah tahrimee – the meat is prohibitively disliked, due to participation in one aspect (catching).
Al-Halwani chose the tahrimee view, indicating it may be the stronger opinion.
4
Contrast with the scenario of the second dog returning prey to itself
The passage notes that if the dog returns the prey to itself (i.e., without a hunter), there is no dislike or prohibition, because a dog's action is not legally comparable to a human's.
This distinction helps clarify that the ruling applies only when a human hunter is involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رده عليه الكلب ولم يجرحه معه ومات بجرحه الأول يكره أكله لوجود المعاونة الأخذ وفقدها في الجرح ثم قيل : الكراهة كراهة تنزيه لأن الأول لما انفرد بالجرح في والأخذ غلب جانب الحل فصار حلالاً وأوجب إعانة غير المعلم الكراهة دون الحرمة. وقيل : كراهة تحريم وهو اختيار الحلواني لوجود المشاركة من وجه بخلاف ما إذا رده عليه نفسه حيث لا يحرم ولا يكره لأن فعل المجوسي ليس من جنس فعل الكلب فلم تتحقق المشاركة من وجه
And if the [second] dog returns it to him [the hunter] without wounding it together with him, and it dies from the first wound, it is disliked (makruh) to eat it, due to the existence of assistance in the catching but its absence in the wound. Then it is said: the karahah is tanzih (reprehensible but not sinful) because the first [dog] was alone in wounding and catching, so the aspect of permissibility prevailed, and it became halal, and the assistance of an untrained [dog] necessitates karahah but not prohibition. And it is said: it is karahah tahrimee (prohibitive), and this is the choice of al-Halwani, due to the existence of participation in one aspect. Contrary to when it [the second dog] returns it to himself [i.e., without any human intervention], where it is neither haram nor makruh, because the action of the Magian is not of the same type as the action of the dog, so participation in one aspect is not realized.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where a trained dog catches and wounds the prey, but a second dog (which may be untrained or a Magian's dog) returns the prey to the hunter without inflicting any wound of its own. The prey then dies from the first wound. The ruling here is that it is makruh to eat the meat, but there is a difference of opinion as to whether the karahah is tanzih (lightly disliked) or tahrimee (prohibitively disliked). The first view holds that since the first dog alone performed the wounding and catching, the prey is fundamentally halal; the second dog's assistance only in returning causes some dislike but does not render it haram. This is karahah tanzih. The second view, chosen by al-Halwani, holds that the participation of the second dog in one aspect (the catching) creates a shared responsibility, so the karahah is tahrimee (close to haram). The passage contrasts this with a scenario where the second dog returns the prey to itself (i.e., without a hunter involved), which does not affect the ruling at all because a dog's action is not considered a legal cause of prohibition or dislike.

Key Terms
كراهة تنزيه (karahah tanzih): A ruling of dislike that is not sinful; it is better to avoid but the act is not prohibited.
كراهة تحريم (karahah tahrimee): A ruling of prohibitive dislike that is close to haram; avoiding it is obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter's trained dog catches a deer and wounds it. An untrained dog then returns the deer to the hunter without inflicting any wound, and the deer dies from the first wound. What is the Hanafi ruling on eating this deer?
A) Halal without any dislike.
B) Haram because an untrained dog was involved.
C) Makruh (disliked), with a difference of opinion on the severity.
D) Halal only if the hunter repeats tasmiyah.

Key Takeaway
When a second dog returns the prey without wounding, the meat is disliked (makruh), with a Hanafi internal dispute on the severity (tanzih or tahrimee).

Extending the Dog's Ruling to Other Trainable Hunters
Extends dog ruling to trainable predators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a trained beast or bird of prey is treated like a dog when it returns game, and identify the condition of trainability for this analogy.

Core Idea
A trained beast or bird of prey is like a dog: returning game without wounding invokes the same ruling.

In this passage, the author extends a previous ruling about a second dog returning game to other animals that can be trained for hunting, such as cheetahs, falcons, and hawks. This analogy is based on the similarity of their hunting action. The passage also touches on animals that cannot be trained, but the text is ambiguous and requires careful reading.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base ruling
Remember the ruling from the previous passage: if a trained dog returns the game without wounding it, the prey is makruh (disliked) to eat. This is the baseline for the analogy.
If you need a refresher, review the lesson on the second dog.
2
Identify the analogy
The passage extends that ruling to any beast of prey (sabu') or bird with talons (dhu mikhleb) that can be trained to hunt. The reason is 'wujud al-mujanasah' – similarity in the act of hunting and returning.
Trainability is key: only animals that can be taught to hunt are included.
3
Apply the analogy
So, if a trained falcon or cheetah returns the game (e.g., after another animal caught it), the same makruh ruling applies as with a dog. The method of returning does not have to be identical – the similarity in the essential action suffices.
4
Consider the exception
The passage then mentions animals that are not permissible to hunt with, like camels, cows, or untrained hawks. The text states they are 'like the dog in all the rulings we mentioned' – but this is ambiguous. It may mean the same ruling applies, or it could be a textual error. In standard Hanafi fiqh, non-trainable animals do not affect the ruling, so the prey remains as it was before the return.
This ambiguity is noted; the reliable position is that only trainable animals are included in the analogy.
5
Synthesize the rule
Practical rule: If a trained beast or bird of prey returns game without inflicting a new wound, eating that game is makruh (disliked) – the same as if a dog had returned it. If the animal is not trainable, the return does not change the ruling.
This ensures consistency across different types of hunting companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رده سبع أو ذو مخلب من الطير مما يجوز أن يعلم فيصاد به فهو كما لو رده عليه الكلب فيما ذكرنا لوجود المجانسة في الفعل بخلاف ما إذا رده عليه ما لا يجوز الاصطياد به كالجمل والبقر والبازي في ذلك كالكلب في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام
And if a beast of prey or a bird with talons, of those which it is permissible to train and hunt with, returns it, then it is as if a dog had returned it to him concerning what we mentioned, due to the existence of similarity in action. Contrary to when that which is not permissible to hunt with returns it, such as the camel, cow, and hawk [in this] – they are like the dog in all the rulings we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling about a second dog to other animals that can be trained for hunting, such as cheetahs, falcons, or other beasts and birds of prey. If such an animal returns the game to the hunter without wounding it, the same ruling of karahah applies as with a dog, because their actions in hunting are similar (they catch and return). However, animals that are not typically trained for hunting, like camels, cows, or even hawks that are not traditionally used in this manner, are treated differently; they are like the dog in all hunting rulings? Actually the text says 'they are like the dog in all the rulings we have mentioned' – this likely refers to the fact that if such an animal returns the prey, the ruling is the same as with the dog? The phrasing is ambiguous. The intended meaning is that non-trainable animals do not have the same effect; they are like a dog in that they can cause prohibition? Perhaps there is a textual confusion. The passage likely means that for an animal like a camel (which cannot be trained to hunt), the ruling is the same as for a dog in the sense that if it returns the prey, it is like the case of the dog? Actually the text says 'في ذلك كالكلب في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام', which seems to say that these animals (camel, cow, hawk) are like the dog in all the mentioned rulings. But that contradicts the earlier distinction: if it is not permissible to hunt with it, it should be different. Possibly the text is acknowledging that the same ruling applies regardless of the type of animal? Let's read carefully: 'بخلاف ما إذا رده عليه ما لا يجوز الاصطياد به كالجمل والبقر والبازي في ذلك كالكلب في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام'. The phrase 'في ذلك' may refer to the ruling of returning the prey. It might mean: unlike the case when something that is not permissible to hunt with returns it, like a camel, cow, or hawk – then in that case, it is like the dog in all the rulings we have mentioned. That would imply that even non-trainable animals, if they return the prey, the same karahah ruling applies. This is confusing. The commentary should note this ambiguity. However, the clear ruling is that for trainable predators, the ruling is the same as for a dog; for others, there is a difference.

Key Terms
سبع (sabu'): Beast of prey, such as a lion, wolf, cheetah, etc., that can be trained for hunting.
ذو مخلب (dhu mikhleb): A bird with talons, such as a falcon, hawk, eagle, that can be trained to hunt.

Game Hunting at the Haram Boundary and the Effect of Apostasy on its Permissibility
Rulings on hunting at the Haram boundary and effect of apostasy on game permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on game killed in the Haram based on the location of shooting and death, and apply the principle that permissibility depends on the shooter's status at the time of shooting.

Core Idea
The permissibility of eating game depends on the shooter's state at the moment of shooting, not the moment of death.

This lesson draws from later Hanafi fatwa collections to address two subtle scenarios: hunting game in relation to the sacred Haram boundary, and the effect of apostasy on the permissibility of game. The key is a principle of legal consideration (al-ʿibrah) that distinguishes between ownership (determined at the moment the arrow strikes) and permissibility (determined at the moment of shooting). Understanding this principle resolves apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Haram-related scenarios
Locate the two cases in al-Fatawa al-ʿAtabiyya: (1) shooting from outside (Halal) causing death inside the Haram, and (2) shooting from inside (Haram) causing death outside. Note the ruling for each regarding permissibility and penalty (jaza').
The penalty is due only when the act of shooting occurs in the Haram.
2
Extend to the dog hunting case
Apply the same logic: if a dog is sent from within the Haram and kills the game outside, the meat is not halal and a penalty is owed.
The action originates in the Haram.
3
Understand the principle of al-ʿibrah
From al-Dhakhira: 'The consideration regarding ownership is at the time of hitting, and regarding eating is at the time of shooting.' Grasp the difference: ownership (who owns the meat) is linked to the strike, but permissibility (halal or haram) is linked to the shooter's state when the arrow is released.
4
Apply to the apostasy cases
Consider a Muslim who shoots and then apostatizes before the arrow hits: the meat is halal because the permissibility was set at the time of shooting. Conversely, an apostate who shoots and then becomes Muslim before the hit: the meat is not halal because the permissibility was set at the time of shooting while he was a non-believer.
This illustrates the principle consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى العتابية : حلال رمى صيدا فأصابه في الحل ومات في الحرم أو رماه في الحرم وأصابه في الحل ومات في الحل لا يحل وعليه الجزاء في الوجه الثاني دون الأول، وكذا إذا أرسل كلبه في الحرم وقتله خارج الحرم لا يحل وعليه الجزاء. وفي الذخيرة : يجب أن يعلم من رمى سهماً إلى صيد أن العبرة في حق الملك لوقت الإصابة وفي حق الأكل لوقت الرمي؛ هذا هو المذكورة في عامة الكتب ولهذا قلنا : المسلم إذا رمى سهماً إلى صيد ثم ارتد والعياذ بالله تعالى ثم أصابه السهم حل تناوله والمرتد إذا رمى إلى صيد أسلم ثم أصابه لا يحل تناوله .
And in al-Fatawa al-Atabiyya: A halal person (non-muhrim) shot an arrow at game and hit it in the Halal (outside Haram) and it died in the Haram, or he shot it in the Haram and hit it in the Halal and it died in the Halal – it is not halal, and he must pay the penalty (jaza') in the second case but not the first. Similarly, if he sent his dog in the Haram and it killed it outside the Haram, it is not halal and he must pay the penalty. And in al-Dhakhira: It must be known that he who shoots an arrow at game – the consideration regarding ownership is at the time of hitting, and regarding eating (permissibility) is at the time of shooting. This is what is mentioned in most books, and for this reason we say: A Muslim shoots an arrow at game, then apostatizes (may Allah protect us), then the arrow hits it – its consumption is halal. And an apostate shoots at game, then becomes Muslim, then it hits it – its consumption is not halal.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage brings two sub-topics from later fatwa collections. First, from al-Fatawa al-Atabiyya: if a non-muhrim shoots game from outside the Haram and it dies inside the Haram, it is not halal (because the Haram sanctity prohibits killing game there). However, the penalty (jaza', i.e., compensation) is only required if the shooting occurred in the Haram. If the shooting was outside but the animal died inside, there is no penalty but still not halal. The same applies if the dog is sent from within the Haram and kills the game outside. Second, from al-Dhakhira: a general principle is established. For the purpose of ownership (i.e., who legally owns the game), the relevant moment is the time of hitting (when the arrow strikes). For the purpose of permissibility of eating, the relevant moment is the time of shooting. This explains a subtle ruling: if a Muslim shoots an arrow at game and then apostatizes before the arrow hits, the meat is still halal because the shooting (which determines permissibility) occurred while he was Muslim. Conversely, if an apostate shoots and becomes Muslim before the arrow hits, the meat is not halal because the shooting was done while he was a non-Muslim. This ruling is based on the principle that the act of shooting is the cause of permissibility, not the hitting.

Key Terms
الجزاء (al-jaza'): Penalty or compensation for killing game in the Haram, which may involve feeding the poor or offering an animal sacrifice.
العبرة (al-'ibrah): The consideration or effective moment for a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim shoots an arrow at a deer while outside the Haram. The arrow hits and kills the deer inside the Haram. What is the ruling on eating the deer according to al-Fatawa al-Atabiyya?
A) Halal, because he shot from outside.
B) Not halal, and he must pay the penalty.
C) Not halal, but no penalty required.
D) Halal only if he retrieves it before it dies.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of game depends on the shooter's state at the time of shooting, while ownership depends on the time of impact.
